Let's build a game with Ruby
Friday night, 1 am. You're down to the last third of your Augustiner and you're frustrated.

For over an hour you've been browsing that folder of unfinished Ruby side projects of yours, realizing that almost all of them are dry CRUD applications. Meanwhile, some of the cool kids in your twitter timeline keep posting their Ludum Dare submissions.

You cannot help but wonder if you should have learnt something other than Ruby.

For a second you even ponder trading in its elegance for some robust C++.

DON'T PANIC. Stop that XCode download. There's hope: Gosu is a Ruby library providing basic building blocks for game development like a window with a main loop, sound samples, gamepad input etc.

And it's so simple that you can even start developing your first game that same night, even after another beer.*

Let me walk you through building a 2D game with Gosu from the first line of code to a playable game.

* [This talk will, of course, neither advocate nor support coding under the influence of alcohol.]


(THIS, THAT, AND THE OTHERS)
// Building simulations beyond the if/thens to the how so’s. 


> This, That, and the Others
I remember it so clearly – driving across the desert on my way to California. The windows are rolled down, allowing the wind to continue its pathway across rather than interrupting to go up and around my small, blue, Toyota SR5 (1984). Up ahead, the first dots of the city begin to add a shine along the horizon – marking the transition from (mostly) vast open expanses of land into modern urban civilization. Once in the city, the transition continues. As we get closer, what appears to be a mass blur of luminescence reveals itself as individual glowing orbs belonging to a city grid. Closer still and we stop at just one traffic light. Beyond it, we can see the subsequent lineup: each taking its turn to move from a dull red to green, and so on, and so on, and so on... It is, in a way, a familiar exercise in everyday powers of ten.1 We’re exposed daily to a dense network of overlapping systems: roadways cutting across flat desert expanses, the domino effect of traffic lights, or airplanes lining up like marathon runners in the changing room before their next big race*. 


It’s hard to imagine a time before such colonization–like the Arctic before the race for exploration began. Our modern environment bears the fruits of a designed world built upon previous technological eras: the extension of information networks established by an Information Age, integrated throughout the backbone of the Industrial Period which came before it. The resulting is a highly structured networked urban environment which extends far beyond the screen: the grid of city streets, the network of traffic lights, the flow of airplane traffic overhead.


The structure is so ubiquitous, that it’s almost unintelligible: media-rich cityscapes and websites now so commonly experienced that we often overlook not only their existence, but that of the underlying creative tools with which we designed them (likely an ever-growing variety of urban planning and media design tools and an equivalent to Dreamweaver, respectively). These tools, in turn, have been designed built upon other models of organizing information, as is reflected in their organizational structure (whether it be paths, landmarks, and structures2 ; or lists, indexes, and directories3 ). 


With the prediction of a not-so-distant future affecting both macro (by 2050, 75% of the world’s population will be living in cities4 ) and micro (Moore’s law predicts a long-term trend of infinitely decreasing technological size matched by increasing processing power5 ) scales, the future looks to be even more structured. Situated within the context of what looks to be an increasingly structured urban future, This, That, and the Others examines the paradox of mediating infinitely subjective experiences through finite logic-based structures. The promise of an increasing hybrid between digital and physical space asks for a redefinition of common understandings within an Information Age such as information itself and related algorithmic processes. 
This paper reviews the process of exploration which helped form the series of methodologies and programs which make up the thesis project,This, That, and the Others: Unconditional Programming beyond the “if/thens” to the “how so’s”. Inspired by theories which suggest that the next stage of technological advancement might champion increasingly modular structures, This, That, and the Others builds upon the observation of current trends for semantic exploration (practiced mainly throughout artistic fields of practice) across the many finite micro structures which underly macro scales. The final projects take shape as speculative scenarios, or programs, executed using the affordances of low-barrier design tools. The implications of such a methodology are inherent to each program, suggesting considerations for more modular structural engineering, as well as the facilitation of complex user experience throughout techno-urban networks. The 5 steps of a method for Unconditional Programming suggest the possibility for facilitating the ongoing evolution of conditional relationships: 1/5: Expand, 2/5: Build, 3/5: Run. 4/5: Array, 5/5: Data. Low barrier tools assist in these explorations, using the affordances of existing technologies to imagine the potential across physical space–“beyond the if/thens to the how so’s”. Given the infinity of potential combinations, and our subjective relational understandings, this Post-Information Age will ask for us to search through our mass structured world and find information as it is meaningful to us.


> Urban Information and Human Processing
The evidence of information in an urban context, for example, is pervasive: apartment numbers, postal codes, detour routes (the list goes on)*. The complex layering of urban systems (both digital and physical, designed and naturally occurring) calls for a broader definition of terms usually isolated to digital space. The subsequent production, aggregation, and analysis of mass quantities of “urban information” is mediated through the constant filtering of information ordering structures, or what could be considered algorithmic codes.6 These algorithms are not necessarily digitally mediated, they might be evidence of other established information structures, such as the organization of beer into neatly packaged assortments of either 6 or 12 - packs or a case of 24. 


Other algorithmic processes such as those used to conduct the flow of traffic are highly networked; built upon the backbone of industrial infrastructures which were later adapted with information networks.7 Designed to create order within disorder, the underlying linear logic of algorithmic codes inherently objectifies the subjective. They set up rules to search through and to generalize information into categories so as to find that which can be logically defined to either fit a defined rule, or to stand outside of it.8 The success of these logical algorithms lies in their objectivity, defining processes of representation which ultimately communicate machine understandings through 0s and 1s.9 In contrast, human logic is highly subjective – the culmination of multiple qualitative experiences subject not to one set logic, but rather to rationale inherent within our individual understandings. Just as algorithms are informed by various “programming languages” and their respective syntaxes, we facilitate our understandings through natural language(s), containing our own biases in a daily process of information processing.10 
One person’s tendency, for example, might be to read a building as aesthetically beautiful; while another might view it as “too modern”. Our biases are informed by our experiences. In effect, the schemas by which we have learned to organize our procedural human understandings inform rules by which we aggregate data, categorize like or unlike things, and translate everyday stimuli into informed conclusions.11 The code, for example, supporting a network of traffic lights does not notice if someone is in a rush; but rather if someone is, or isn’t present.* A pedestrian, on the other hand, might inform multiple conclusions from their observations. They may infer, for example, that a woman tapping nervously on her steering wheel could mean that she is late, or that she is enjoying dancing to her music. 




> The Strength of Human Rationale
The strength of our logic lives within this subjective qualitative reasoning: the speculative, the inexpressible, the intuitive, and the narrative. This is, in essence, that which can more easily be written about or illustrated. The written works of urban novelists China Miéville or Italo Calvino demonstrate the full potential of their freedom in exploring the urban environment. In his novel, The Scar, Miéville describes a floating ocean city constructed from the amalgamation of ships:


“They were built up, topped with structure, styles and materials shoved together
from a hundred histories and aesthetics into a compound architecture. Centuries-old
pagodas tottered on the decks of ancient oarships, and cement monoliths rose like extra
smokestacks on paddlers stolen from southern seas. The streets between the buildings
were tight...Decktop houses were cracked and strained from the boats’ constant motion.”12


In order to explore information processing inherent within the everyday experience, I began a series of exercises which I called “humans writing programs”. Participants were asked to respond to a variety of prompts such as “write a story of a recent interesting experience in code”, or, “write a list of anything”*. The prompts were written as lists of information on simple lined notebook paper. I reminded participants to separate ideas as they saw fit and to number each line as they progressed.


The ongoing work titled Sorted Books by Nina Katchadourian illustrates the nature of procedural human understandings informing the categorization of like things. She collects a manual assortment of books gathered from various libraries:


“...ranging form private homes to specialized public book collections...[where] the process is the same in every case: culling through a collection of books, pulling particular titles, and eventually grouping the books into clusters so that the titles can be read in sequence, from top to bottom*. Taken as a whole, the clusters from each sorting aim to examine that particular library’s focus, idiosyncrasies, and inconsistencies — a cross-section of that library’s holdings.”13


With the “algorithm” she has identified (based on her own interpretation of sequence and categorization), this piece makes a lovely point of the strength of human intelligence over computer intelligence.


> Lost in Translation
The freedom of intuitive expression through natural language doesn’t quite translate to machines. They first ask for a breakdown of information, often which requires the construction of multiple algorithms to parse through machine understandings.14 A recent workshop with the graphic design studio Lust demonstrated this process.15 Given the task of using Processing to develop a data visualization from the transcripts of a Republican debate, I decided to aggregate the information by assigning visual values to represent how “family-friendly” the candidates are*. This required the generation of multiple supporting information structures, including: the parsing of debate data into a spread sheet, the construction of functions to assign different classes to different types of information, and accessing keyword text files of words assigned with negative or positive values.


The co-founder of Area/Code, Kevin Slavin, describes how this kind of parsing of data affects the natural process of human expression: 


“ You’re no longer writing to communicate clearly with a human. You’re writing to
be as clear as possible to Google search algorithms. It’s creating a kind of writing
that denigrates language. But, it makes perfect sense; with SEO-driven sites, their
audience isn’t an idealized human reader, it’s an idealized machine reader — which has
quantitative criteria*. [In this search-driven landscape, we’re adapting to] an algorithmic
approach to English... It’s a way to parse language precisely, in unambiguous terms,
without the subtleties and vagaries of genuine human speech.”16




Donna Haraway would describe this as the establishment of increasingly cyborg systems, and the resulting production of outsider figures. Outsiders are those who haven’t met the designed system of built society, and who in effect (either by choice or circumstance of legibility, etc.), are left to either adapt within, or flee outside.17 Inspired by the desire to flee from what he considered to be the “over-civilization” of industrial society, Henry David Thoreau escaped to find refuge on the banks of Walden Pond in a two year experiment of simple living.18 It might be difficult to envision the possibility for this kind of refuge in the future, where the prevalence of infinitely expanding structure may not allow for entire escape. This is true on a macro scale with the prediction that by 2050, 75% of the world’s population will be living in cities.19 Moore’s law outlines the probability that this might be true on the micro scale as well, suggesting a future of ubiquitous embedded technologies which are likely to be invisible to the human eye.20 
This question seems especially relevant now given what is happening with the Occupy movement, and corresponding calls to modernize and re-evaluate the finite and broken codes of mass-systems supporting economics, politics, and education.21


Given the potential unlikelihood of entire escape, This, That, and the Others envisions the possibility of adapting infinitely subjective interpretations within existing finite structures. Writers like Gertrude Stein and Anaïs Nin are two examples of those who adapted the most basic technology of their time – the written language – to allow for intuitive human expression.22 The result of their experimentations were great – taking creative liberties to extend the traditional one-to-one relationship between syntax and semantics. Stein’s words, for example, suggested the subjectivity of relationships across multiple viewpoints.23 Does the inherently logical structure of our current algorithmic codes prohibit necessary intuitive exploration and subjective adaptation as practiced by the likes of Gertrude Stein with natural language? With the prediction of an increasingly structured future urban environment, what are some possible avenues for adapting the finite nature of algorithms to allow for the integration of infinitely subjective perspectives?


Inspired by my own observation of a fast-evolving urban structure within my own hometown of Pittsburgh, I wrote a manifesto entitled 38 Lines: A Dreamer’s Manifesto in Code*. I attempted to capture my desire for fulfilling the exploratory intuition which I see in myself as well as other Pittsburgh transients who have migrated east from their previous homes (in successfully developing places like Portland) after hearing word of the “potential” in underdeveloped cities like Pittsburgh.24 Throughout this exercise, I borrowed from my own limited understanding of the conditional programming language, exploring the potential for intuitive expression within the constraint of “fixed” systems such as the syntax of “If’s” and “Then’s. I found refuge in the snippets of natural language storytelling possible within comments beneath the coded syntax.


Oh, the irony: within the infinite potential of algorithmic structures, we are contained to finite structures of practice. In effect, this restricts contribution of various perspectives to those insiders who are already fluent. A future of increasing urban structure is paired with an increase in diversity.25 As such, it will be increasingly important to continue to explore the full potential of our technologies: the aggregation of large amounts of information, and the integration of human strengths for intuitive exploration and infinite semantic interpretations. 




> Possibilities within a Post-Information Culture
In their new book Collage Culture, essays by Aaron Rose and Mandy Kahn consider how this kind of subjective interpretation might be exactly where we can find ourselves as result of an information culture in which we feel overwhelmed with abundance. They suggest that we create by collaging, or re-editing where “[our] signatures [are] embedded in ... subtle acts of choice” rather than “creating art from scratch.” The collages of Brian Roettinger which sit in between the two author’s essays translate a combination of 25 compositional rules into a series of 16 compositions*.26 


RULES FOR COMPOSITION 1–10
1 — Use 20%, 50%, 60%, or 70% white space
2 — All elements must touch each other
3 — Use one image multiple times, at varying scales
4 — Use a white background
5 — Compose twelve lines, all of different lengths and widths
6 — Fill the background with a pattern
7 — Fill the background with an image
8 — Include a line between one and eight points in thickness
9 — Scale image at 200%
10 — Scale image at 20%


This trend for collaging would seem evident to the avid internet surfer who has first-hand witness: a handful of “meme” related sites27, the color-based search site False Arms28*, or doppelgänger personalities on Facebook.29 How might this collage mentality translate beyond momentary trends, and offer possibilities for integrating wider perspectives within finite algorithmic structures?


Consider the common example of pattern recognition within computer terminology:  x == y (non-coders might be more familiar with the mathematical syntax “x=y”)30. Its function is to search for the “equivalency” of two things. In a world of ubiquitous information, there is potential for multiple semantic relationships while lie beyond the limitations of finite rule sets which assign a quality x as identical to a variable y. The possibilities suggest the promise of exploring infinite opportunities for pattern recognition, subject to interpretation on part of the viewer. For example, I might say an apple == an orange (as they are both fruit), while someone else might say an apple == lipstick (if seen as both red in the mind of the interpreter).


Playing off of the familiar game of Memory, I developed “Pick a match, Any match”*, as a way to explore an exercise where “x=y, but what are ‘x’ and ‘y’?”. This exercise acts as a proof of concept of the potential for applying human processing power to filter through mass groups of information within a Post-Information Age. In exploring new connections, we are likely to borrow from other structures. We could borrow, for example, literary terms such as metaphors and idioms to rationalize interpretations of foreign relationships between a deer and lamppost (“a deer in headlights”*) or a clock and a wing (“time flies”*).


In an urban environment, stimuli occurs naturally in the information transmitted throughout everyday experience. The experience of seeing the night sky demonstrates the subjectivity inherent in an experience sure to occur at the intersection of multiple overlapping designed and “natural” systems. Natural forces of nature culminate in what we perceive to be twinkling stars, and are layered with the effects of pollution affecting visibility; all of which are witnessed by one viewer from one point in space (given the surrounding environment facilitates the view), and their internal and external conditions (they may or may not consider a plane to be part of a night sky). 


Guided by a curiosity of how complex human experience might translate back into executable, logic-based code, I met with programmer Casey Anderson and brought with me my pile of jumbled observational system “glitches” I had compiled into a “Not Yet Catalogue”*. I pulled one out of the pile and asked him to help me develop a code for “the experience of seeing a bird and a plane at the same time”*. I recorded him speaking, as he drew the sketch below*. 


A discussion in coding quickly turned to a philosophical discussion. It became clear that there were many ways to interpret this experience (any of which would result in other aspects getting lost in translation). We explored multiple options of how to code the experience:


<search for 2 things which:
share the same function, but different parameters
// both an airplane and a bird fly, but one is natural and the other is mechanical
>
or
share the same function and the same parameters, but different variables
// both an airplane and a bird fly in the sky, but one is slow and rhythmic and the other
comparatively fast and steady multiple iterations.


In their project Sealand, Metahaven considers their role as design detectives facilitating a “form of contemporary myth-making” which is not:


“[based] on the a priori truths about the organizations that conventionally form the starting point for corporate identity projects [but rather to uncover the] ... epic story about Sealand ... embracing an infinite amount of possible Sealand definitions and stories [as searched through google search and everyday objects]*, multiplying and re-ordering them by self-defined principles into an identity of unprecedented richness.”31


> What is the potential for facilitating a rich re-ordering of post-information semantics on an urban scale? 


Although this might sound like an unrealistic stretch of the imagination, algorithms are increasingly driving not only our digital information structures, but information networks throughout the systems of our built environment: our cars, our homes, our neighborhoods, and our cities.32 I looked to Christopher Alexander’s Pattern Language (1977) for inspiration. Influenced by the developing understanding of network structure (which evolved as a way to discuss computer programming and design), A Pattern Language breaks down the complexity of a built environment into 253 patterns, which together form a “pattern language”*.  Alexander’s motivations were to extend the ability for coding and decoding the built environment beyond the professional realm, to ordinary people.33


> Reverse Engineering Urban Experience
In order to explore the potential for a kind of matrix of “code-able” understandings within existing systems (so as to allow for their adaptation and the exploration of their execution), I adapted the traditional method of engineering development. Engineering is described as the application of “scientific, mathematical, economic, social, and practical knowledge in order to design and build structures, machines, devices, systems, materials and processes.”34 I borrowed instead from reverse engineering, a process (originally used for military methods) which allows for the ability “to deduce design decisions from end products with little or no additional knowledge about the procedures involved in the original production...[and]...without using or simply duplicating the original.”35  


The proposed method started with end experiences within everyday urban living, using observation and secondary research to decode them into a kind of “view source” for higher literacy and editing. The resulting were a set of pseudo-algorithmic blueprints or experience maps which identified their constituent variables and qualifying parameters with x’s and y’s*. The aim was to suggest the potential for semantic reordering before executing back into the urban context.


Currently, reverse engineering is used for many applications: generating computer-aided designs (CAD), protein simulations in nanotechnology, and across a wide array of other computer-aided simulations.36 This reverse engineering process was applied across a loose definition of algorithmic codes within urban experiences, including: frameworks guiding aesthetic decisions such as the arrangement of chairs in a dentist waiting room, or the alignment of trees within landscape design*; highly designed networks such as the flow of airplane traffic*; even predictable moments of seeming serendipity such as a balloon caught on a telephone wire*. 


Some were executed by coding a quick interpretation in Processing, exploring the translation of cross-relational human “information” back into executable code*. As I progressed with the experiments, I considered further inquiry of the implications of executing semantic re-orderings within open-source engineering processes. 


> Could simulation environments help gain understanding regarding the resulting implications of adaptable engineering on aesthetics and culture?  


The following example simulating a future algorithm for defining flags*, for instance, suggests how a complex algorithm containing smaller dynamic constituent components might reflect influence on the cultural identity of its corresponding countries.


> How is this different than customization? 


Some might argue that this kind of integration of individual perspectives within systematic processes is already being practiced as customization. Customization, however, maintains the abstraction of the initial input. It is often interpreted and translated by a skilled worker, or rule-based algorithmic process which simply allows higher variability within the end product.37 By comparison, a process of adapted reverse-engineering processes could consider the realization of “everyone as a maker”.38 The potential of individual interpretive coding processes could reveal participants’ corresponding influences, biases (as they may borrow from various choice formats for structuring information) and choices of coding “languages” (this means writers writing in gmail as well as coders writing in processing, etc.).


In the following experiment, I asked my brother (a biophysicist familiar with coding proteins using Python) to code his interpretation of a traffic light. Asked to document his process within the comments, the resulting visualization demonstrates the inherent biases and choices within his chosen coding practices: “a set of four traffic lights, each set to display a default color of red, unless the keys a,s,d, or f are pressed...*”


> What other methods might be employed next to integrate observations of a post-information age or to imagine speculations of a near future influenced by post-information mentality, and the effects as executed across a highly structured urban context?


The following experiments will continue to apply a process of reverse engineering to decode observations of modern urban experience. The resulting will inform a kind of storytelling through the generation of “pseudo-code” scripts*, and the playing out of film scenarios*.


Firms use a combination of debt and equity to finance their assets. The proportion of debt and equity determines a whole hierarchy of fixed claims on assets and earnings, with premiums paid for different levels of risk. “How do firms choose their capital structures?” Debt will oblige the firm to make contractual fixed interest payments in each period to the debt-holders, where equity allocates claims on the free cash to equity. Enterprises can choose from a various range of financial instruments to finance its investment. Debt can be obtained by issuing bond or by taking a loan from a bank either short or long term. Hybrid instruments, such as convertibles combine characteristics of debt and equity. Equity might have the form of common or preferred stock. Capital structure ratios describe the debt level in relation to the value of the enterprise, in terms of market or book value. The actual corporate debt ratio is defined as the value of debt (D) divided by the value of debt and Equity (E): In the last decades a large amount of empirical research examining the determinants of capital structure has been done, making the debt to equity ratio the dependant variable and bringing more clarity into the capital structure puzzle. “We now understand the most the important departures from the Modigliani and Miller assumptions that make capital structure relevant to a firm’s value.” Rajan and Zingales (1995), examining leverage in the G-7 countries, see capital structure influenced by country specific factors, on one side. Corporate financing decisions depend on institutional differences, like the tax and bankruptcy code, the meaning of corporate control, the historical role played by banks and security markets and inflation. On the other side they see leverage correlated with firm specific factors, such as growth opportunities and the expected cost of financial. They use size as a proxy for the probability of default and use the market to book ratio as proxy for growth opportunities. Titman and Wessels (1988) use six measures of financial leverage. They are long term, short term and convertible debt divided by market and book ratio. Harris and Raviv (1991) mention that “leverage increases with fixed assets, profitability and firm size and decreases with volatility, advertising expenditure, the probability of bankruptcy and uniqueness of the product.” They give an overview about models explaining capital structure. One argumentation string is based on agency costs and sees conflicts between equity holders and managers or conflicts between equity and debt-holders as determining the financing behavior of firms. Models based on asymmetric information describe firms following a Pecking Order, managers using debt to signal to the market or firms timing debt or equity issues to the market conditions. All models on capital structure rely on the assumptions of Modigliani and Miller, describing the value of a firm as a function of its expected cash flows. The modern theory of capital structure has its roots in the framework delivered by the path breaking article of Modigliani and Miller in 1958. “What is the "cost of capital" to a firm in a world in which funds are used to acquire assets whose yields are uncertain; and in which capital can be obtained by many different media, ranging from pure debt instruments, representing money-fixed claims, to pure equity issues, giving holders only the right to a pro-rata share in the uncertain venture?” The question raised by Modigliani and Miller in 1958 on the cost of capital, has not lost its relevance. The M&M capital structure irrelevance proposition has set the theoretical framework for modern corporate finance research and empirical analysis. Furthermore, the M&M propositions have led to advanced theoretical models of capital structure, leading to both clarity and controversy. Indeed, many economic researchers have followed M&M´s path, developing various theories and models to specify the determinants of capital structure. The question on whether there is an optimal capital structure remains an important one barred with endogenous and exogenous convolutions that suggest interrelationships among existing models as well as correlations between a general model formulation and resulting large and complex structures; the emergence of global economical structures further impacting upon the topic’s complexity.  “That is, the average cost of capital, to any firm is completely independent of its capital structure and is equal to the capitalization rate of a pure equity stream of its class.”  The fundamental idea of M&M was to characterize a firm as a production function of its capital streams and differentiate firms with regard to risk classes. They pointed out under which conditions capital structure is irrelevant to the value of a firm and that the market value of a firm should depend on its expected capital streams. 30 years after their influential paper, Merton Miller admits: “Showing what doesn´t matter can also show by implication what does.” The original M&M propositions are valid under these conditions: A perfect capital market allows all informed participants to lend and borrow money with the same interest rate. Investors and firms have equal access to financial markets. As a result, investors can determine leverage according to their needs. There are no transaction costs.  In a frictionless world, a firm would be indifferent in issuing securities as equity or debt. The absence of bankruptcy costs. No asymmetric information and no agency costs, firms act in the best interest of its shareholders. Firms can be divided into "equivalent return" classes. This implies that shares within the same class differ by a scale factor and the ratio of the return to the expected return is identical for all shares in the class and the share price is proportional to its expected return. No tax subsidies. Modigliani and Miller start with the assumption, that a firm has a specified set of expected cash flows and capital structure decisions in order to finance its assets and divide up the cash flows among investors. A firm would invest in a physical asset if it increases the net profit of the owners of the firm, under the condition that the yield of the asset exceeds the rate of interest. Furthermore, an asset should be acquired, if it adds more to the market value of the firm than its costs and increases the value of equity. The value it creates can be defined by capitalizing the stream it generates at the market rate of interest. The existence of uncertainty superimposes a risk discount to be subtracted from the expected yield or a risk premium to be added to the market rate of interest. “Because firms may have different proportions of debt in their capital structure … the shares will be subject to different degrees of financial risk or "leverage" and hence they will no longer be perfect substitutes for one another.” In the Proof of Proposition I by M&M,  denotes the market price of a share of company j.  denotes the expected return realized by the company. Therefore,  is the expected return per share. In a first step, a factor of proportionality is introduced to the model. denotes the expected rate of return of equity for all firms j in class k under all equity financing. To prove the first proposition,  is introduced to describe the market value of debt and  denotes the market value of equity. Proposition I now asserts that the value of a company can be defined equally by the sum of market value of equity and debt or by capitalizing its expected return at its appropriate rate. This means that the market value of any firm is independent of its capital structure. The value of a firm therefore will be determined by capitalizing its expected return by the appropriate rate of return in its class. “That is, the average cost of capital, to any firm is completely independent of its capital structure and is equal to the capitalization rate of a pure equity stream of its class.”  can be stated as the firms average cost of capital, which is the ratio of its expected return to the market value of all its securities. To establish their proposition, they show that arbitrage opportunities arise if the relation (2) or (3) does not hold. In the example, there are two companies. One is unlevered, while the other one has an amount of debt. Both companies have the same return X. An Investor is holding s2 dollars worth of the shares of company 2. The return from his portfolio is denoted , which will be a fraction  on the return X, less interest charge. Now, the investor sells his fraction  of shares of  and takes a bank loan   pledging his securities of new shares of  as collateral. So he gets  shares of . Comparing (4) to (5), we see that as long as we have  we must have. M&M conclude therefore, that levered companies cannot command a premium over unlevered companies, because investors could put the equivalent leverage in their portfolio by taking a personal credit. Since arbitrage will prevent  from being larger than, M&M conclude that in equilibrium we must have , as stated in Proposition I. From proposition I M&M derive the following proposition II concerning the rate of return on common stock in companies whose capital structure includes some debt: the expected rate of return or yield, i, on the stock of any company j belonging to the kth class is a linear function of leverage as follows: “That is, the expected yield of a share of stock is equal to the appropriate capitalization rate  for a pure equity stream in the class, plus a premium related to financial risk equal to the debt-to-equity ratio times the spread between and r.” Under the assumption that a firm is acting in the best interest of its shareholders, it will exploit investment opportunities if the rate of return of the investment is larger than its average
 cost of capital. Every project with a positive NPV, discounted by the weighted average cost of capital will be undertaken by the firm. In 1963, M&M admitted that, taking corporate income taxes into account, the debt to equity ratio might affect the market value of a company: “The market values of firms in each class must be proportional in equilibrium to their expected returns net of taxes (that is, to the sum of the interest paid and expected net stockholder income).” Taking the existence of tax payments into account, it can be shown that "arbitrage" will make values within any class a function of the tax rate and the degree of leverage. Instead of they introduce  as the rate at which the markets capitalize the expected returns and the tax rate. The value of a levered firm, therefore, will be  plus the value of interest tax shields. We see that the firm value is increasing with the corporate debt level. The value of the company would be maximized under all debt financing. Introducing tax effects, M&M define required rate of return on equity: The tax corrected version predicts a smaller increase of the risk premium with higher leverage as a matter of tax deductibility of interest payments. After introducing a tax component, M&M derived: Taking tax benefits of debt payments into account the weighted average cost of a firm is a decreasing function of its debt to equity ratio. is the return on equity for the unlevered firm and the market value of the unlevered firm. The decrease of WACC with leverage shows that the value of a firm is not independent of the debt/equity division in the capital structure, due to the deductibility of interest. A decrease in the weighted average cost of capital would increase free cash to the firm and therefore the market value of equity. “The optimal capital structure might be all debt.” Companies with too low debt levels and high bond ratings were leaving too much money on the table in form of unnecessary corporate income tax payments. The prevailing academic idea by the mid 70´s was that the optimal capital structure involves balancing the tax advantage of debt against the present value of bankruptcy costs. Miller introduced a model showing that the tax advantage of debt financing might be offset under certain conditions by the tax disadvantage of interest income from debt at a personal tax level. The following models introduced various leverage related costs with explanatory power to determine optimal capital structure. Cost of financial distress, agency costs and non debt tax shields became subject to further theoretical approaches, also flexibility to finance future investments became an issue. “A firm's optimal debt ratio is usually viewed as determined by a tradeoff of the costs and benefits of borrowing, holding the firm's assets and investment plans constant. The firm is portrayed as balancing the value of interest tax shields against various costs of bankruptcy or financial embarrassment.” According to Myers (1984), a firm that follows the trade-off theory sets a target debt-to-value ratio and then gradually moves towards the target, which is determined by balancing debt tax shields against costs of bankruptcy. A firm´s optimal capital structure is determined by a tradeoff of costs and benefits of its debt ratio. The theory says that firms substitute debt for equity or equity for debt until the value of the enterprise is maximized and the target ratio is reached. The fact that actual debt ratios, observed by empirical studies, show wide variation could possibly be explained by adjustment costs. Random events might bump them away from the optimum and force companies into long excursions away from their optimal ratio. On the other hand, if adjustment costs are small and firms would be close to their optimums, differences in risk and other variables must be explanatory for the variations in capital structure of firms. The prevailing academic view that the optimal capital structure balanced the tax advantage of debt against the present value of bankruptcy costs was challenged by Miller (1977), questioning the relevance of bankruptcy costs. Comparing bankruptcy costs to tax savings on interest payment seems to be relatively small. He delivers the argument that equity holders can benefit from leverage by taking personal taxation into account. Where is the corporate tax rate, the personal income tax rate applicable to income from common stock and the personal tax rate on income from bond, B is the market value of the levered companies debt. When the tax rate on income from shares is less than the personal tax rate on income from bonds, the gain from leverage will be smaller because the before tax return on taxable bonds has to be higher to offset this disadvantage. “Any situation in which the owners of corporations could increase their wealth by substituting debt for equity (or vice versa) would be incompatible with market equilibrium. Their attempts to exploit these opportunities would lead, in a world with progressive income taxes, to changes in the yields on stocks and bonds and in their ownership patterns. These changes, in turn, restore the equilibrium and remove the incentives to issue more debt, even without invoking bankruptcy costs or lending costs as a ´deus ex machina´.” Since then new models were developed to reconcile Miller´s model with the balancing theory of optimal capital structure. The main finding being that there are significant leverage related costs, such as bankruptcy costs, agency costs of debt and loss of non debt shields. De Angelo and Masulis (1979) follow thåe path of taking personal taxation into account and extent Miller´s analysis. They find that Miller´s irrelevancy theorem is extremely sensitive to realistic and simple modifications in the corporate tax code: “Specifically, the existence of non-debt corporate tax shields such as depreciation deductions or investment tax credits is sufficient to overturn the leverage irrelevancy theorem.” Their model implies the existence of a unique interior optimum leverage decision for each firm in the market equilibrium after taking all supply side adjustments into account. They exclude bankruptcy, agency and other leverage related costs, admitting that their presence will determine the optimum capital structure. Their model focuses on the relation between the debt level of a firm and available level of non debt tax shields. Bradley et al.(1984) develop a single period model upon this idea. “The model captures the essence of the tax-advantage-and-bankruptcy-costs trade off models, the agency costs of debt arguments, the potential loss of non-debt tax shields in non-default states, the differential personal tax rates between income from stocks and bonds.” The value for the firm that can be created by the use of interest tax shields depends on several factors. Interest tax shields increase the value of a company only if they will be fully used. Higher non debt tax shields, such as depreciation and amortization and tax credits, will increase the probability of wasting interest tax shields. Therefore, De Angelo and Masulis propose a model that takes different possible states of earnings into account, affecting the actual value of debt  and equity: For earnings greater than s2, tax shields can be fully used and increase the value of the enterprise and value of equity. For earnings smaller than s2 but larger than s1 the value of the firm will be the face value of debt plus a fraction of the value of the tax shields, determining the value of equity. This possible outcome shows that an increase in NDTS would decrease the need for debt tax shields, keeping volatility of earnings constant. For earnings smaller than s1, the company will find itself in financial distress, the value of equity will be zero and the value of debt a fraction of the face value of debt. The loss to debt-holders can be described by: . The current market value of the firm is determined by sum of the actual market value of debt and equity:, taking into account the vectors of state contingent. Bradley et al.(1984) introduced to the formula of DeAngelo and Masulis personal income tax considerations to adjust the market value of debt and equity. Bradley differentiates the equation V with respect to B the promised interest payments to bondholders, setting B as a first order condition. The market value of the firm is maximized when equals zero. The first term in this expression represents the marginal net tax benefits from debt.  The second term represents the probability of wasting tax shields. The third term represents expected cost of financial distress. The market value of the firm is maximized when equals zero. The model allows re-differentiating the first order condition in respect to each parameter and shows: The equation shows that trade off theory basically involves three aspects determining leverage decisions: An increase in the cost of financial distress (k) reduces the optimal debt ratio.Higher non debt tax shields (  reduce the optimal debt ratio. Increasing the personal tax rate on income from equity increases the optimal debt level, debt decreases the optimal debt level. The figures depict the results of the simulation of the firms optimal leverage decision. Sigma, on the horizontal axis describes differences in volatility. Each line in the figure depicts the functional relation between the optimal debt ratio and the variability of the firm’s value, for different levels of k and NDTS. The optimal debt ratio is inversely related to the variability of firm value, if costs of financial distress are significant. Asymmetric information describes the state that two contractual parties do not have the same information about the contractual agreement. Information asymmetry is a necessary outcome when information is confidential and needs to be kept
 from competitors, i.e. on one hand. On the other, educating investors takes time and money. The existence of so called information costs is unavoidable for any firm. Furthermore, moral hazard and adverse selection may result from information asymmetry. Akerlof (1970) described adverse selection as a problem of uncertainty. The manager of a firm usually knows more about the real value of its assets and investment opportunities than the investors. As a result some issues will be under or overpriced. The Lemon problem describes that market participants adapt their behavior and would predict a reduction of quality, as a result of information asymmetry. Moral hazard, from an economical point of view arises from information asymmetry, when one contractual party bears less of the downside risk, but has more information about the actual state - a conflict that may arise between bond and stockholders is studied in the context of the principal agent problem. Adverse selection problems will lead to conflicts between old and new or inside and outside shareholders. The introduction of this topic set the framework for new approaches to explaining capital structure. The basic idea is that only inside managers know the true value of a company’s assets and growth opportunities. The manager’s perception of the relative value of its assets to the market value would determine the choice of issuing equity or debt. The outside investor can only interpret this signal and assume that managers’ thinking their company is overvalued issue equity. Managers, acting in the best interest of its shareholders try to mitigate the problem of assets being underpriced by following a pecking order. The underlying idea of the pecking order theory was already observed and clearly expressed by Donaldson (1961): “Management strongly favored internal generation as a source of new funds even to the exclusion of external funds except for unavoidable ´bulges` in the need for funds.”The term of pecking order was established by Myers and Majluf (1984), defining the financing behavior of a firm, following a pecking order: Firms prefer internal finance, such as retained earnings and will adjust their dividend payout policy to their investment opportunities. If external financing is required, firms will choose the safest type of fund first. They prefer debt, than hybrid securities, such as convertibles, and as a last resort issue new stocks. According to the concept of information asymmetry, there might be some cases where managers even pass up valuable investment opportunities by refusing to issue new shares. This is in the state, where the costs to old shareholders of issuing new shares outweigh the NPV of the investment project. The pecking order hypothesis, therefore, sees the interests of the management and current shareholders aligned. Managers are forced to rely on internal cash flow because of information asymmetry between themselves and potential new shareholders. Investors might interpret the decision not to issue shares or to issue debt as a good sign and see it as an indicator for quality of the firm. Baskin (1989) sees in addition to the effect of asymmetric information, tax and transaction costs to be in favor of a pecking order explanation of corporate financing behavior. Direct costs of retained earnings might be smaller than those of issuing debt, banker´s fees, i.e. Second, the personal income tax payments on income from dividends by the investors can be reduced if retained earnings are used to fund new investment projects. In the previous paragraph, capital structure emerged as a result of the problem of asymmetric information. The signaling model focuses on the personal behavior of managers. According to Ross (1977) managers know the true distribution of expected returns. Thy benefit from increasing the market value of the firm´s securities and will suffer in the case of bankruptcy. By issuing debt the risk of bankruptcy increases for the firm. Managers will issue debt, in order to benefit from the increase of the market value of the firm, only until the point where the personal penalty for the managers, related to the increase in the probability of bankruptcy outweighs the benefits. Therefore a higher proportion of debt is regarded by investors as an indicator of quality. Since marginal expected bankruptcy costs are higher for low quality firms, they will not imitate high quality firms by issuing more debt. High quality firms are therefore allowed to take a higher portion of debt. The main empirical result is that debt to equity ratio is positively related to profitability. In Ross model, quality of the firm, debt level and bankruptcy probability are all positively related. A model by Heinkel (1982), similar to the previous mentioned, suggests that high quality firms have a higher value of equity, but lower quality bonds (lower market value for a given face value). Signaling being a high quality firm by issuing debt will increase the market value of equity on one side but decrease the market value of debt on the other.  An additional explanation of the differences in capital structure can be given by the market timing framework. The market to book ratio of equity is used as indicator if a firm is over or undervalued. The hypothesis says that firms tend to issue equity when their market value is high relative to book value and repurchase equity when market value is low. According to this concept, the capital structure of a firm is affected persistently. The empirical study by Marsh (1982) focuses on how companies select between financing instruments at a given point in time. He finds that the decisions of issuing equity or debt by a company are heavily influenced by market conditions and the past history of security prices. This is essentially a behavioral model, implying the preference of the management issuing equity in times of strong performing stock markets and issuing debt when interest rates are low. Analyses of long-run stock returns also give evidence to the market timing theory. Firms tend to issue equity when the cost of equity is relatively low and repurchase when the cost of equity is high. Analyses of earnings forecasts suggest that those firms tend to issue equity when investors are too optimistic about future growth. Surveys of managers are consistent. Baker and Wurgler (2002) ask how market timing affects capital structure. Their results suggest that it has persistent effects on capital structure. In their empirical study leverage is the dependent variable and market to book ratio the independent one. They find that low leverage firms raised funds when their market to book ratio was high while high leverage firms raised funds when their m/b ratio was low. The basic regression result is that leverage is strongly negatively related to this measure of historical market valuations. “But in view of the fact that it is usually argued that co-ordination will be done by the price mechanism, why is such organisation necessary? Outside the firm, price movements direct production, which is co-ordinated through a series of exchange transactions on the market. Within a firm, these market transactions are eliminated and in place of the complicated market structure with exchange transactions is substituted the entrepreneur-co-ordinator, who directs production.” In 1937, Ronald Coase posed the classic transaction cost problem: When do firms procure in the market and when do they produce to their own needs? He argues that differences of transaction costs are mainly responsible for the decision to use markets or hierarchical forms of organization. The transaction cost approach defines a firm as a governance structure, assuming that the behavior of its participants is only limitedly rational and sees incomplete contracting as a consequence. Contractual hazards are the result of self-interest seeking and opportunistic agents. With reference to the limited rational and opportunistic behavior TCE examines alternative forms of economic organization to reduce transaction costs caused by incomplete contracting and contractual hazards. Williamson (1998) proposes to treat debt and equity not only as alternative financial instruments, but rather as alternative government structures. The proportion of debt to equity should principally depend on the characteristics of the assets of the firm. Transaction cost reasoning supports the usage of debt governance, which establishes strong rules, to finance re-deployable assets and equity governance, allowing much greater discretion, to finance non-re-deployable assets. In this sense, a higher debt to equity ratio limits the decision making authority, concerning the strategy of the firm and further investments i.e., of the equity holders and the managers of the firm. Agency theory considers an organization as a nexus of contracts and refers to moral hazard and agency cost, instead of opportunism. The separation of ownership and control is regarded by the classical agency theory. New institutional economics literature mostly focuses on the normative aspects of the organization. Jensen and Meckling (1976) define an agency relationship as a contract under which the principal engages the agent to perform services on behalf of the principal, by delegating decision making authority to the agent. The Agency problem assumes, if both parties are utility maximizers that the agent will not act in the best interest of the principal. To limit resulting divergences of his interest, the principal will establish appropriate incentives and a monitoring system to reduce the aberrant activities. So called bonding costs are to ensure that the agent will not take actions which would harm the principal. In addition there will be some decisions of the agent, not maximizing the welfare of the principal. This difference is referred as the
 residual loss. The sum of those monitoring and bonding expenditures and the residual loss is defined as agency costs. Agency cost increase with the degree of separation of ownership and control.As mentioned before, agency costs appear because of the separation of ownership and control. If ownership and control are in one hand, there are no agency costs. Jensen and Meckling distinguish between different forms of agency problems. First they analyze the effect of outside equity. If the owner manager of a firm sells some of his equity claims, his proportional claim on the revenues falls, which will encourage him appropriate corporate resources in the form of perquisites, since he will only bear a fraction of the costs. Furthermore, the owner’s incentives to dedicate a significant effort to find new investment opportunities and to maximize the value of the company will also decrease. Since minority shareholders will realize and align incentives, invest in monitoring and expect a residual loss, the agency costs will be reflected in the price they are willing to pay. These agency costs are an unavoidable result of the agency relationship and mainly absorbed by the owner. The owner manager will try to keep the agency costs small and only accept to create this relationship if benefits are high enough to outweigh them. Benefits can arise from the availability of profitable investments. Jensen and Meckling, given the existence of agency costs and the discretionary power of managers of large corporations, find it difficult to understand the historical growth in equity. Limited liability seems to be one additional attractive feature of the corporate form. But if this would be all, why don´t we find most companies individually owned with a tiny fraction of equity and the rest debt? Expanding an enterprise by new investment projects and growth opportunities opens the possibility to design a whole hierarchy of fixed claims on assets and earnings, paying premiums for different levels of risk. Jensen and Meckling see mainly three factors, associated with the existence of debt, influencing the decision how to set up this framework: High leverage engages owner managers to engage in activities with a high pay off, but a low probability of success. If it succeeds he captures most of the gains, and the creditors bear the risk. By promising the bond-buyers a low variance project, with stable cash flows and then taking a high variance project, reduces the market value of debt and increases the market value of equity. With increasing debt to equity ratio, the risk aversion of the (controlling) equity holders decreases, because the present value of returns on equity is higher on risky, volatile projects than the present value of loss. To limit the managerial behavior bondholders include various covenants in the indenture provisions, reducing the value of the bonds. Provisions include constraints on dividends, future debt issues and maintenance of working capital. Practically, it is difficult to protect bondholders and to limit the riskiness of projects in most operating aspects of the enterprise. Monitoring costs include writing and enforcing those provisions. Furthermore, the ability to take optimal actions by the management is sometimes reduced. Bondholders will engage in monitoring costs until the nominal marginal costs are equal to the benefits.Bankruptcy occurs when a current payment on a debt obligation cannot be met by the firm. Equity holders loose all their claims and debt-holders bear the difference between the face value of their claims and the market value of the firm. Liquidation will occur when the market value of future cash flows will be less than the marketable value of the assets. Bankruptcy is not costless and consumes a fraction of the remaining value. The value of debt is related to the bankruptcy costs and to the probability of bankruptcy. Jensen and Meckling believe that bankruptcy costs are relatively small and not a major determinant of capital structure. Mergers might be used to avoid bankruptcy, reorganization costs are much smaller. But revenues and operating costs are not unaffected by the probability and the cost of bankruptcy. Executives might get a higher salary to bear the risk of unemployment. Also business partners have an interest in a low bankruptcy probability to build a sustainable business relation; this type of cost related to the probability of bankruptcy might depend on the industry and the position in the supply chain. Debt will be used as a source of financing if the marginal agency costs of debt are lower than agency cost of equity and because of tax benefits. A high degree of outside financing increases the amount of total agency costs. The agency costs of debt are zero when no debt is used and the agency costs of outside equity, when no outside equity is used. The combination of debt and outside equity can reduce the total agency costs. Jensen and Meckling assume that there is a point where an optimal combination of debt to outside equity minimizes the total agency costs of outside financing. If a higher portion of outside financing decreases or increases the optimal debt level, is speculation. Jensen (1986) points out that control effects of debt are a potential determinant of capital structure. Increasing the debt to equity ratio affects the organizational incentives to motivate managers and reduces the resistance to retrenchment. The obligation to make debt payments increases the organizational efficiency. Debt decreases the discretion of managers to spend free cash flow and thus reduces the agency costs of free cash flow. As discussed before, leverage has also agency costs of debt. Therefore the optimal debt to equity ratio is reached at the point where the marginal costs of debt offset the marginal benefits. The control effects of debt issues will be not as important for high growth companies with low free cash flows. Those companies have to acquire capital from the financial markets, which role is to evaluate the company, its management and projects. The control function of debt is more important for organization with large, stable cash flows, but low growth opportunities and even more important for companies, which must shrink. In this context, it has to be mentioned that growth increases managers’ discretion and power. Therefore managers have incentives to grow beyond the optimal size and increase resources under their control. Their compensation is also positively related to growth in sales. On the contrary, competition in the market requires managers to reduce average costs and increase efficiency to enhance the probability of survival. Harris and Raviv (1991) find that a larger debt level improves the liquidation decision because it makes default more likely. Under all equity financing, managers will try to keep the firm running, even if the assets are more worth than the present value of future cash flows or their liquidation value. Under this assumption, an optimal capital structure trades off higher investigation costs against improved liquidation decisions. Their model predicts, that firms with lower investigation costs and a higher liquidation value, e.g. a higher proportion of tangible assets will have more debt and are likely to have a higher firm value.  Reputation is mentioned to moderating the asset substitution problem, where levered equity holders choose risky investments to benefit from large gains with bearing only part of the risk. Managers of a company with a long history of not defaulting will be more likely to choose safe investment projects rather than maximizing shareholders equity. Young firms with little reputation might be more likely to choose risky projects, and after surviving they might change their behavior. Long established firms, with a long track record, might choose the low risk project in order not to lose valuable reputation and benefit from low interest rates and will have a lower probability of default. Managers might also tend to choose the low risk project not to lose their personal reputation. An explanation is given by Hirshleifer and Thakor (1989), the managerial labor market would more distinguish between success and failure, rather than valuing the maximization of expected return. This managerial behavior reduces the agency costs of debt and allows companies to increase the debt ratio.

 
 Hundreds

STAGE VISUALS, 2014

Concept and design of stage and stage visuals for Hamburg based electronic pop duo Hundreds. Technical concept, pre-production and support on tour as video operator.

Ongoing project with more than 150 shows since 2011 in Germany, France, Netherlands, Belgium, Luxembourg, Poland, Italy, Austria, Switzerland, Sweden, Lithuania, Estonia, Iceland, UK and USA.

We did concept, design and animation. We also developed the projection setup which was meant to be as unique as possible and at the same time flexible enough to fit into venues with very different layouts and sizes.

The challenge was to considerably enrich the concert experience while not outshining the band's performance. Our personal goal was to establish stage visuals as abstract as possible but at the same time always with a strong and clear link to the lyrics and music.

Tamed Virus

GENERATIVE STAGE VISUAL, 2015

Words of a Middleman

INSTALLATION, 2012

»Words of a middleman« is a Wireless Router analysing network connections in a local network and displaying them on its integrated display.

APPROACH

Wireless routers are a connection to the internet and thereby a local hub to our digital communication. With their help we send emails, connect to other devices and surf the web. In carefree interaction with our computers, we think the network traffic is handled calm and confidential by the wireless router. In this space of digital intimacy the wireless router is a silent observer. It demonstrates the intangible nature of digital data and we perceive the router as a symbol for our connection to the internet. In this major role as middleman in the digital communication he doesn't get enough credit, in form and content. The project »Words of a middleman« describes a scenario, where the wireless router steps out of its shadow existence and communicates actively his role.
Inspired by the narrative style »stream of consciousness« the router emerges as a narrator, observes the events within the network and journalize them publicly. Devices, servers and their users act as performers equally and are in steady dialog with each other.

CREDITS

Collaboration with Christoph Steinlehner and Lino Teuteberg

The project was supervised by Jonas Loh and Steffen Fiedler at the Interface Design program of the University of Applied Sciences Potsdam.




Aperture

MEDIA FAÇADE, 2012

Programming of a controller software for animations on a media façade consisting of 120 irises.

The installation’s custom-made diaphragms similar to those found in cameras are arranged in a grid and form a membrane. A façade covered with these electrical eyes becomes a porous skin, allowing the world to flow through it.

Conscape

RESEARCH AND DATA VISUALIZATION, 2011

Concept, design and programming of a data visualization on a multi-touch wall display. The prototype was built as an exploration tool of Berlin’s live music scene on an interactive map. It allows to browse all of Berlin’s gigs and spot trends over time.

Each venue is represented by a circle varying in size and color, depending on the amount of visitors and the genre of the artists playing there. By dragging the timeline users can travel through history. Long-term trends as well as weekly and seasonal patterns can be spotted. To get a closer look, the data can be filtered by genre or details on specific clubs can be called up.

Ambient Intelligence

INSTALLATION, 2014

Concept, design and programming of a game involving the player’s whole body for the DASA work exhibition in Dortmund.

The exhibit gives insights into how today’s technology is already able to track the human body. Measuring posture and movements for example can be helpful to ergonomically optimize work environments.
The game technology is based on the Microsoft Kinect 3D sensor, showing real time information of the user being tracked. Certain joints, changing in every level and appearing as measure marks, are being used to create a shape. Through different poses, the visitor can change its form and size. As the shape has dropped to the virtual back wall the user gets challenged to jump through its inverted successor.

CREDITS

Collaboration with TheGreenEyl

Exhibition design: Franke | Steinert




Creeping Things

INSTALLATION, 2009

Design, illustration and programming of an interactive installation for Jewish Museum Berlin.

»Ye shall not make yourselves abominable with any creeping thing that creepeth, neither shall ye make yourselves unclean with them, that ye should be defiled thereby.«
The Book of Leviticus

A massive mirror, populated with insects generated by a computer greets visitors in a room of the »Kosher & Co« exhibition. Creeping and crawling they surround the reflections of the humans.
As people leave, the bodies of the insects come back together to form words: a quote from the Book of Leviticus that refers to the imperative of cleanliness in Jewish tradition.

I'll be home for Christmas

DATA VISUALIZATION, 2012

The visualization of US flight data evolved during 72 hours of Visualizing Global Marathon 2012 and won in the infographic category.

The main idea was to relate the given data set to worldwide recurring events and customs like holidays and vacation times as well as to exceptional events like 9/11. Looking at the data this way we could find both certain patterns and disturbance of these patterns during the last 20 years.

In the end we generated a set of information visualizations and gathered them into a poster named »I'll be home for Christmas«. The challenge was to find a certain question, that could be answered with the data set of worldwide flight traffic, and to create a proper design of these insights within two days of a very intense weekend.

The original poster of this version can be found here.

Metacognizer

RESEARCH, 2011

An experiment to find new perspectives on the so far strongly scientifical analysis of brainwave data in collaboration with the »Cluster Languages of Emotion« of Freie Universität Berlin.

A human's emotional condition is measured through electroencephalography (EEG) as the balance of a human being’s concentration and relaxation. The data streams’ are being audio-visually translated and then output into the test environment as projections and sound. While simultaneously seeing and hearing his visualized brain waves, the participant reflects his own brain activity. He is influencing the reaction of his emotional state’s representation in a resonating feedback loop.
We asked ourselves: What are the consequences of these kinds of reciprocal effects?

Similarly to the generated moiré-like visual structure the sound generator produces overlaying sinus waves, which get placed and rotated within the room. According to the mood of the proband, the system creates different patterns of sound and imagery, merging and rotating at varying speeds.

A highly immersive full dome projection environment can dissolve any reference points which possibly cause severe perception-changing effects. Furthermore its multi-channel audio system allows sound to be placed at any desired point within this space. A strong influence on the emotional state of the participant and a mesmerizing experience can be created, resulting almost in a feeling of virtual weightlessness.

CREDITS

Collaboration with Daniel Palmér and Robin Rundkvist

The project was supervised by Willy Sengewald at the Interface Design program of University of Applied Sciences Potsdam. Additional supervision by Matti Gärtner of Cluster Languages of Emotion at FU-Berlin.
