End Pilot

MUSIC FESTIVAL, 2004 – 2009

This is how it started, our first project: idea, concept and realization of a music festival. We were young, naive and strong-minded. A small group of friends who didn't want to accept the fact that we had to travel a couple of hundred kilometres to see our favourite bands in concert. So, in our home town, we started our own festival and concert series disregarding genre boundaries and paying lots of attention to details.
After an exhausting but very rewarding five years it came to an end, with all of us leaving for new places and challenges. More than twenty concert nights took place, including performances by artists like Nils Frahm, Apparat, Slut, Polarkreis 18 and Kangding Ray.

CREDITS

Collaboration with Martin Pleiß, Maxie Fischer and Sandra Wolff


Ethical issues on cyborg technology: Diversity and inclusion

Enno Park

Revised version

Cyborgs e.V.
Lychener Str. 74, 10437 Berlin
Berlin, June 10, 2014
Discussion note

Ethical issues on cyborg technology: Diversity and inclusion

by Enno Park

Progress has reached the point, where cyborg technology is leaving the sphere of mere science fiction. Whereas society as a whole has formed a symbiosis with technology long ago, individuals are now starting to merge with technology as well. The effects can already be studied on the example of smartphones, computers, and the internet. The ideas of „repairing“ humans, of medical implants exceeding natural abilities, and even of upcoming non-medical implants lead to a lot of ethical questions and to re-thinking the concept of inclusion.

More than 300.000 people worldwide have an artificial ear ii NIH Publication No. 11-4798 (2013-11-01). "Cochlear Implants". National Institute on Deafness and Other Communication Disorders. - they wear a cochlear implant, that provides mostly satisfactory hearing to deaf or nearly deaf poeple as long as they had developed the necessary brain structurs in their early childhood. The success of the adaption of the implant varies from person to person. E.g. in my case, the understanding of spoken language increased from 25% with hearing aids to 100% with cochlea implants measured in a quiet environment without background noise. When observing the development of prosthetics one could state that human beings become more and more „repairable“. Concerning my case, it was simply that: a repair of my defect hearing after 22 years of nearly deafness. Because technology has become part of my body, I identify myself as a cyborg, though I reckon that only a small group of the disabled persons wearing prostheses feels the same way, even though technology has become a part of their body just as well.

The term „cyborg“ was first created to describe a cybernetic organism based on „altering man's bodily functions to meet the requirements of extraterrestrial environments.“i ii"Cyborgs and Space," in Astronautics (September 1960), by Manfred E. Clynes and Nathan S. Kline. The term was extended when Donna Haraway identified the breakdown of three boundaries in the last century: between human and animal, between organic lifeform and machine, and between physical and non-physical.i iiiCf. "A Cyborg Manifesto: Science, Technology, and Socialist-Feminism in the Late Twentieth Century". Simians, Cyborgs and Women: The Reinvention of Nature. Routledge. ii For the first boundary Sigmund Freud showed that humans not only act rationally but driven by instict and subconsciousness. For the second boundary, modern brain resarch and genetics recognise humans more and more as a kind of biological machine. For the third boundary, we still cannot grasp the phenomenon of spirit and mind, even though the development of artificial intelligence using neuronal networks for pattern recognition has made a big progress. In accordance with this ambivalence society as a whole has formed a symbiosis with technology decades ago and can be seen as a cyborg society, even though the individuals seldom see themselves as cyborgs. In the last years technical devices and computers have not only become smaller and smaller, but also closer and closer to the body. Smartphones and wearables are ubiquious today, and from an engineer's point of view the implantantion of electronics is mostly reduced to the question of how energy can be supplied inside the body.

As a conclusion from this development, I prefer to use the following definition: A cyborg is a human being with an electronical device implanted in or steadily attached to the body with the purpose of increasing individual senses or abilities beyond the occasional use of tools. Cochlea implants are one of the first devices that come near to complying with this defintion of a cyborg and thus mark a milestone in the history of prosthetics. Unlike most prostheses they are not longer worn as a tool or garment, but are implanted in the body and connected to the nervous system. When it comes to prostheses, there is a wide variety from tool-like wearables—such as ancient wooden legs, which are used only when needed, but not necessarily while sleeping or working at a desk—up to devices that keep up vital body functions—such as pacemakers. Normally, these prostheses don't increase senses or abilities beyond a normal extend. Cochlea implants, however, profoundly alter natural hearing; they make it programmable. Whereas for a healthy person the world sounds the same every day, persons with cochlea implants can control their hearing. Via remote control they can raise or lower volume and choose between several presets for different environments such as specifically optimizing for understanding spoken language, filtering environmental noise, amplifying very faint sounds, or disabling any filters for full musical enjoyment. It is standard to change the frequency range of the device or to map certain frequencies into other areas of the range. This way even hearing ultrasound is theoretically possible with a reprogrammed cochlea implant.

This variable hearing conveys an interesting parallel to another aspect in Donna Haraways work: the „situated knowledge.“iiv "Situated Knowledges: The Science Question in Feminism and the Privilege of Partial Perspective". Haraway, Donna. Feminist Studies Vol. 14, No. 3. pp. 575–599. 1988. With this term Haraway describes knowledge that can be true or false on the same object depending on the particular situation, and tries to cope with the limitations of the human perception from a post-structural point of view. This abstract topic in science criticism becomes relevant for cyborgs in a completely non-abstract way: the more cyborgs can control and vary how their senses adapt to nature and environment, the more they will experience how much their concept of the world depends on their senses and point of views. In enhancing and controlling the senses lies a chance to have a broader view on the environment and feel closer to nature as a simple, non-abstract every-day experience. In spite of some losses compared to natural hearing the cochlea implant exceeds normal senses, which makes it a „cyborg device.“

Usually, implants are only used for medical purposes, but the first hacker groups have started to invent small implants such as magnets in order to feel electromagnetic fields, RFID-tags and NFC-chips in order to connect an implant to a mobile device, and small sensor boards in order measure body functions. At the moment, this trend is far from mainstream, but when you keep in mind how many people are willing to modify their body for aesthetical reasons—e.g. tattoos, piercings ans cosmetical surgery—the integrity of the body seems to be dependent on culture. Non-medical implants could become mainstream quickly as soon as they provide useful benefits in comparison to wearables. Currently, it is hard to outline what such a device would look like—comparable to predicting the smartphone one hundred years ago when the classic telephone just started to prevail.

This trend brings up several ethical issues on body integrity, control, security, and above all social inclusion. Discussing these issues is no longer a gedankenexperiment; it has already arrived in our society today. When athletes with prostheses started to outrun their non-disabled competitors, the question of „techno doping“ arose in the field of sports, marking cyborg technologies as creating unfair advantages. vvFelix Gillette (10 November 2004), "Racing tall: A Paralympian stands accused of getting an illegal leg up", Slate When the first Google glass surfaced, an activist group called „Stop The Cyborgs“v vihttp://stopthecyborgs.org/ started to distribute signs for restaurants to prohibit the use of the devices in their establishments in fear of being photographed or filmed without their knowledge. And the fact that the trend is taking place both in the medical and in the non-medical realm shows that these issues concern our society as a whole.

Most peoples' first reaction to the merge of human and technology brings up the fear that their body's integrity could be injured. I, however, don't see that as a serious problem, because, as mentioned above, we already have a culture of  non-medical body modification in significant parts of our society. More important seems to be the question of control and security. Recognising that a person can be a cyborg entails that technical devices are treated like body parts, e.g. confiscating a smartphone could be valued as physical injury in the future.

At the moment, medical implants are restricted medical technology and can only be adjusted by experts. This limitation exists for good reasons. Misusing the tools for fine-tuning and reprogramming devices like cochlea implant can lead to serious damage for the the cyborg. As a result these cyborgs have no opportunity to manipulate their devices and try out new applications, for example to be able to hear ultrasound, as mentioned above. They also have to trust vendors, clinics, health insurance, and even the government that their devices are not misused. For instance, one can imagine health insurance companies using surveillance or collecting data to apply different insurance fees depending on a person's behavior.

The situation is already tenuous when it comes to computers, smartphones, and other devices ranging from quantified-self measurement to home automation. We are surrounded by sensors and leave a broad data track with whatever we do. Black hat hackers and viruses threaten vital infrastructures, vendors control which apps we are allowed to install on our devices or remotely delete data from e-book readers, and several secret services spy on computers and the internet traffic all over the world. With cyborg devices becoming mainstream, all those problems would aggravate severly and could have the potential to comprimise our body and even vital life functions.

Except for the drastic solution of simply stopping the use of digital devices—implanted or not—, open source software and hardware could offer a way out of the dilemma. Publicising the source code and construction plans of the device seem to compromise security, but any security issue can be seen as a bug that can be eliminated in a process of peer review by independent experts. In addition, theoretically everyone who is willing to delve into the workings of their device, is able to find new and surprising applications.

Furthermore, implants exceeding a human's natural senses and abilities bring up the next ethical problem: inclusion and the social question. People argue that gaining extra abilities with cyborg devices could lead to a bigger gap between rich and poor. In my opinion this is a trivial thought and not neccessarily a consequence of technology but a political question of distribution. Unequal access to goods, education, and career opportunities is a problem that existed far before the digital or even the industrial revolution and can only be solved on another level.

An interesting effect of cyborg technologies is that they uncover subtle discrimination and a lack of inclusion in society. While prostheses are meant to offer help, disabled persons sometimes feel them as a kind of performance pressure to fulfill the needs of society and economy. In many discussions  I experienced that a feeling of „not being good enough“ or not being accepted the way they are is common among many handicapped persons. E.g. the deaf culture sees itself threatened by the cochlea implant for several reasons. In the first years, cochlea implants weren't good enough to provide a satisfactory understanding of spoken language. People who are born deaf lack the brain structures to decode sounds and spoken language, and very few of them are able to learn it as adults. All that led to the image of the cochlea implant as a poor solution. This took place in the context of the rich culture deaf people have developed around the sign language.  This culture was suppressed by the doubtful educational practice in European schools for the deaf by forcefully eliminating the usage of sign language.v viiSturley, Nick (2010). "Eight Resolutions". Milan 1880; http://milan1880.com/milan1880congress/eightresolutions.html ii Even though this happened in the well-intentioned approach to make the children fit for a hearing world, the deaf often developed a feeling of being declined by society as what they were. Deaf people fear that their culture could cease to exist when most children grow up with their sense of hearing owing to a cochlea implant. Some more radical spokespersons of the deaf community already call the cochlea implant a „cultural genocide.“ vviii Cf. „Pediatric Cochlear Implants: Medical Miracle or Cultural Genocide?“ by Holly Heffley, University of Kansas School of Nursing, http://archie.kumc.edu/bitstream/handle/2271/848/STT-JUNW_2010_Heffley_Pediatric-Cochlear-Implants.pdf?sequence=1

Also non-disabled people face interferences from outside. Society is thwarting peoples' attempts to modify their bodies for non-medical reasons. One will have difficulties to find a physician who is willing to implant non-medical cyborg devices into the body. Such operations are regulated by ethical commissions and would impinge the hyppocratic oath in most cases. This leads to the side effect that self declared cyborgs have to sidestep to piercing studios, which could increase the surgical risk due to lower standards compared to medical surgery. This is ironic considering that nobody will encounter problems to find a cosmetic surgeon for purely aesthetical reasons.

Inducing disabled persons to undergo surgery for implants while at the same time deterring non-disabled persons from altering their bodies is nothing else but the attempt to normalize individuals. That is the opposite to the thought of inclusion, which is, in a nutshell: providing barrier-free access to social life for any person with any disability as far as possible. This thought had effect on countless laws worldwide and the UN convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities, esp. Article 17 „Every person with disabilities has a right to respect for his or her physical and mental integrity on an equal basis with others.“ iixCf. „Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities“, Office of the High Commissioner on Humans Rights, United Nations x Cyborg technologies are often associated with Star Trek's „borg collective“ and co-optation. The social trend to normalization leads in the excat direction that critics of cyborg technologies fear.

On the contrary, cyborg technologies offer the chance to understand inclusion as a continuum cointaining individuals with a wide variety of abilities and disabilities without defining a norm. For, any norm excludes people, which then have to be re-included with great effort. In addition, the concept of a normalized human being goes hand in hand with the idea of a human as a repairable machine. Most disabilities are far too complex to be „repaired“ as simply as hearing loss. The idea, that anyone could be repaired leads to less efforts on inclusion and, in the long run, to discrimination when a disabled person is not able nor willing to have technical implants. A cyborg is not an ubermensch, but simply another person with abilities, disabilities, individual experiences, and cultural peculiarities just like humans always have had.

ii "Cyborgs and Space," in Astronautics (September 1960), by Manfred E. Clynes and Nathan S. Kline.

iii Cf. "A Cyborg Manifesto: Science, Technology, and Socialist-Feminism in the Late Twentieth Century". Simians, Cyborgs and Women: The Reinvention of Nature. Routledge.

iv "Situated Knowledges: The Science Question in Feminism and the Privilege of Partial Perspective". Haraway, Donna. Feminist Studies Vol. 14, No. 3. pp. 575–599. 1988.

v Felix Gillette (10 November 2004), "Racing tall: A Paralympian stands accused of getting an illegal leg up", Slate

vi http://stopthecyborgs.org/

vii Sturley, Nick (2010). "Eight Resolutions". Milan 1880; http://milan1880.com/milan1880congress/eightresolutions.html

# Nokia to drop Cellular providers

The actual title in the article in Ars Technica is "Cellular providers want Nokia to drop Skype from cell phones". Two cell service providers in the UK are supposedly up in arms over Nokia's inclusion of Skype software on its N97 handset, and are threatening not to carry the device unless the software is ditched. This stance is not only annoying to consumers who are beginning to like VoIP, but it could also even hurt the carriers' business in the long run. I find this pretty upsetting, and makes me want to rewrite the sentence to say the opposite: "Nokia to drop Cellular providers".

My sentence is of course just an exaggeration, but I wish it could happen. I find the prices of cell phone calls and messages very high compared to the price of data transmission over DSL or cable. The thing is, I could have an iPhone touch with WiFi or some other portable device where I can install any software I want. Then I could send and receive any data I choose, including bidirectional audio (Voip, like Skype for example), video calls, or text as long as I want, not just 160 characters. That's pretty much unlimited monthly transfer without paying for each call or message. No abusive charges for roaming either. There are projects like the Fon - Skype WiFi phone that let you talk for free to other skype contacts anywhere where a wireless connection is available.

I recently experienced this. A friend bought an EeePC and flew to a city in the other side of the world. She called me from that city using Skype from different locations. The computer found available wireless networks while walking around. When she called me I could hear and see what was around, thanks to the webcam. It was a surprising experience. No contract, no dialing numbers, not limited to a few characters, better quality than mobile or land line phones. It felt like live tv transmission. Of course I've used webcams before, but when you can see good quality video from the street it's something else. Some would argue that's stealing someone's connection. But they are free to put a password for it. I know people who leave it open on purpose. Sharing connections is the base for projects like Fon or Freifunk.

Of course cellular providers will fight that you are able to send messages and call from out there without paying their fees. But I think that when fat connections are available anywhere, these companies will have to change or disappear. I don't understand why aren't they smarter and see the possibilities and try to innovate offering something new before other's do. Instead, they try to hold strongly on to what they have now. I guess they just try to get the biggest number of clients, and to get the most money out of these clients, without considering so much which product would make the most sense to the customers. I've seen this myself where I've worked before.

In these days you can have a flat data plan on your phone, and push-email. That means that if you receive an email, you are immediately notified. So your friends can send you 'sms' (actually an e-mail, not limited to 160 characters) for free. If both you and your friends have such data plans and cell phones, you wouldn't need to send sms anymore, saving some money. Normally cellular providers forbid using Voip in this situation. But just because they want to charge more for the same service. It would not cost them more to allow it, but people would stop calling using their networks. That's how I think it should be in the future. Flat rate data transfer anywhere.

# At Skizum Studios

The first Sunday of May 2009 I gave a small talk at the Skizum Studios in Berlin. Scott Bolden had the great idea of inviting two creative persons every two Sundays to describe projects or interesting facts.

For me it was quite an improvised happening. I'm not used to talking in public, and that was one of the reasons I wanted to do it. I talked about Finland, Mallorca, nature, waves in the sea, curiosity, science, the "hydrogen bomb" I created when I was a young kid, computers, programming, randomness, measuring power consumption, testing battery charge, harbors, interesting states of mind, happiest moments in life, being lost, paragliding, inspiring events, creativity, math, among others.

I also showed some creations, like my home page, the sound gun, a concert I did, photography and more. You can find links to some of those creations at my home page. There was no time to explain many other things I had written down, like this sound game I did in my job at Panfu last year, which was inspired by Toshio Iwai's work. I like thinking about interfaces.

I also didn't have time to mention that I've been helping with the programming of the website www.ae911truth.org It's a website where many architects and engineers ask for a new investigation of what happened 8 years ago in New York, and has a 2 hour video presentation of many interesting facts. Or that soon I will visit Uganda and develop phone applications to help doctors in developing countries. I plan to describe this journey in this blog, alternating English and Spanish.

Here are links to some inspiring events you may have a look:

Online conferences at TED (see "Rated jaw-dropping" on the left).

Transmediale. Event in Berlin, in January/February each year. There I saw lots of interesting things, like Syscapes #interlude, and Recipes for Disaster.

Recordings of the Chaos Computer Club conferences in Berlin, each year in December: (some very geek, others not so much, like 2916 Wikileaks)

OFFF: another inspiring event I attended in 2006. Tickets are always sold out very quick. There were so many amazing things shown there. This one from Golan Levin and Zachary Lieberman is one of them (see the whole playlist in YouTube!).

I hope that in the future I take some time to write posts about all the subjects I mention above, and upload some other creations I did not present at the Skizum Studios.

Thank you everybody for attending!
I hope you enjoyed it :)

# Pick Up Spore - Live 2007

Taas tuli tyhjäks - earth  by  hamoid

This is the recording of my performance in Mallorca. This was meant to be played in the Pitch Festival, but it was cancelled last minute, so I played in an open air bar next to the cathedral (Ses Voltes). I find this piece as a movie without images. It includes different types of sounds: field recording I did using SoundMan binaural microphones outdoors, sometimes I mounted the recorder in my SoundGun:

Those microphones are normally mounted in your ears, and they record the true 3D sound as we humans hear it. The 3D effect is so real that, when using headphones, I kept turning around when I heard my cellphone beep to realize again that it was coming out of my headphones and not from my phone.

The piece also includes synthesized sounds, classical instruments, and non conventional instruments too. To me the track represents life on this planet, including beauty, rain, destruction, money...

# Back to 52ºN

Friday we spent the day packing our luggage and our data. We shared photos, transfered video tapes and copied the files we've been working on. The way to the airport was quite interesting. We left the house at sunset time, when a big traffic jam takes place every day. Even there are no traffic lights and cars move slowly, I think drivers don't get stressed. Edward always seemed very calm. I haven't seen people screaming or honking. Actually I haven't seen anyone agressive at all.

Right now I hear people honking all the time here in my street in Berlin because they have to drive slower than usually. Today Fete de la musique takes place and some streets are for pedestrians only, so cars have to drive other routes. Sometimes someone even screams. Why are they so stressed?

While driving to the airport I found it sad to see a woman sitting on a dusty sidewalk grilling corn to sell. She was there with her small son that tried to climb on her. The kid smiled and laughed, and she played with him. Around her there was some trash. The corn was quite black after a long time in the small grill. I wonder if someone was buying any of it. It's strange that it felt sad for me while they seemed happy.

Edward took a different route to avoid the traffic jam. We drove on some dirt streets with huge holes and bumps. The car's bottom often hit those bumps. I saw the night life in Kampala. There were some pickup trucks with loud speakers, pool tables, bars and lots of people hanging out there. It was a busy and very interesting area. http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kabalagala

In the photos, a mobile card shop. Then Sarah, Miquel with Conejo, Solomon, Hakim and I, and finally someone being driven around in a bicycle on the way to the airport.

It was amazing how bright the stars were on the road to Entebbe. You could see some even near the horizon.

Tuesday 60ºN :)

# Big heavy bird

This afternoon, in the trees in front of our house, two big birds were sitting in the trees. Because of their weight, the branches where they sat were bending down. They were quite slow, not like small birds which normally jump and look around nervously all the time. These birds turned their heads slowly. Sometimes they began looking slowly down, to then jump to a lower branch. Other times they jumped sideways repeatedly on one branch, until the weight was too much for the tip of the branch. They ate (or at least scratched) part of the outside of this eucalyptus tree. They seemed to be a couple, probably an old one. It didn't look like they just met, but anyways when one flew to another tree, the other followed.

# Conference recording hardware

This post is for geeks :)

Today we tested a device that will be used to record lectures at the university. The device is a computer with a built in touch screen. This computer has two video inputs, one for a video camera that records the person giving the speech, and another video input for the laptop of the presenter. The device records sound, the presenter, and his slides. The system automatically does OCR in the slides, and it indexes the text, so it can be searched afterwards. Then it sends the presentation to the Internet, either in real time or later when a connection is available. The conference is then available for students to be seen online, including an automatically generated time line that includes texts and slides. If the presentation is real time, people watching the conference can interact with the presenter, for example sending questions. It's also possible to upload a presentation to the server from the presenters laptop, using it's own microphone and webcam. The streaming then happens using a RED5 Flash server (open source).

Yesterday by accident the power supply blew up (don't ask), but an indian technician had it back working today in just an hour. Miquel was very happy about the fast reparation. So for the test we connected everything up, showing Miquel's two Ugandan helpers how it all works. I spoke a few words to the camera while clicking through a power point presentation, and then it was all  uploaded to the server almost automatically, where it could be seen in a web catalogue of presentations.

The system will offer a lot of educational content online to students and professionals in the continent. Soon a fat optic fiber will give Africa fast access from one university to another, and to the rest of the online world ( see http://www.seacom.mu/intro.html ) making online education a reality.

Going back to my cube :)

By the way: I haven't seen a single mosquito here... and maybe... a total of one fly? (I guess in the country side it's something different)

# First application demonstration

Today we showed the telephone application to other people for the first time.

In the morning we discussed all the video shooting locations and ideas for the coming days.

The morning was quite a stress, because we only had a few hours to do some changes and fixes to the application and server side, but the Internet was at its worse moment since I'm here. I tried to log in into our server for hours without any luck. We were running late for our appointment, so Bob, Michael and Sal left with one of the drivers. Meanwhile Hakim ran to a shop and got an UMTS stick. He arrived home and in seconds, I plugged the thing, logged into the server, edited some files typing at super speed, closed the laptop and left.

Sal and Michael came from the Bay area to create a video presentation of the project to raise funds. Bob is the director of our project. I heard he was a doctor in India for a very long time. The first video shooting was at Doctor Stella's office. She's the director of the Reach Out project near our place, and she's so nice and friendly! I was touched by her words. She explained the history of her project that takes care of people affected by HIV and TB, their difficulties, how it has evolved over time. Bob and Miquel presented her the phone application and web site to find out what she thinks about it. Her reaction was very positive.

The second video shooting was with two health workers, while some chicken ran under our feet. You can see some photos taken while the phone was presented. Their reaction was also very good. I expected they might be much more skeptic, but they said it could save lives! After filming kids were amazed with the camera, laughing with joy and making silly faces in front of it.

Until yesterday I thought this phone application is a good idea, but it was just software in my test phone and the server. That changed today. Hearing the comments from Doctor Stella and the two health workers made it obvious that what I've been programming can have a real impact in people's life's. I don't know how to describe it, but I'm surprised.

Amazing how many interesting people I meet here :)

# First day

Today I could actually see what's around us (yesterday at night I could not see much). Everything is very green. There are some interesting colorful plants, and very big birds and some eagles flying around. In the morning we walked for half hour to a shopping center where we had breakfast. The cars here drive on the left side, so one must be careful when crossing the street. While walking I passed a children school called "The hungry carterpillar". I also saw a sign saying "beware of the falling mangos" stuck on a tree. After breakfast we checked out the supermarket. Quite big, and with a great variety. Then we both rode a Boda-Boda with a young driver to another shopping center (after discussing the price). There we bought some food, activated the satellite tv contract, exchanged some Euros and had a great mango juice. I noticed some people from India and I asked Miquel about it. He said that many used to live here, but they were kicked out by a past government. In recent years some have returned to the country. A taxi driven by a friendly woman took us back home in a few minutes. She gave us her phone number so we can call her if we need a taxi. Miquel stored it in his personal collection of taxi driver's numbers.

Today is the mothers day in Uganda, so the school in front of our house was closed, and I was told everything was more quiet than usually. Congratulations to all mothers :)

Then we started working in the project. My part is to program cell phones that run Google's Android operating system. I was doing some tests during the last weeks in Berlin using a phone emulator, but today it was the first time I run a program in a real G1 phone :) The internet was going really slow, but it looks like maybe we solved it (deactivating QoS).

During the day I took some photos from the roof-balcony, I recorded the noise from the fan in my bathroom, I ate a great tasty mango, had dinner in an Indian restaurant (our driver took us there, 5 minutes away from the house) and had many long interesting conversations with Miquel about our lives, being true to oneself, technology, people, the time he worked at MSF in north Uganda...

Time now to enter my cube and rest until tomorrow morning. Good night all!

Un abrazo para Ricardo!

# From the equator to the Nokia country

I noticed a extreme change in what surrounds me during the last days. The streets, the people, the air, the sound, the temperature... it's another universe.

I've seen people with red (almost fluorescent) hair, a Gothic couple with huge platform shoes, black leather clothes with metal pieces attached, black paint in their faces, I've seen punks and large stylish sun glasses... I tried to imagine a Goth in Kampala... would the kids run towards them screaming "muzungu"? or would they run away screaming something different?

I imagined explaining someone that you can buy broken jeans that look old in Europe that cost as much as one months salary in Uganda, and that extra effort is taken to break them and wear them out. I'm pretty sure they try to take much care of their few clothes down there.

I also imagined showing these different groups of people to one of the persons I met last weeks in Uganda and then asking "in which of these styles would you like to dress?". The idea of a custom party (or mask party) comes to my head.

Funny how I can feel at home in such radically different places after such a short period of time.

# In Kampala

Finally here :) it's 22:30. My trip started at 04:30, when a taxi picked me up in Berlin. The way to the airport was quite weird: the taxi was shaking laterally quite strongly while in the highway. The driver said it was the road, but I don't believe it. I've driven there before and my car did not shake like that. I thought the wheels were going to come out. This was Berlin, not Kampala :)

Later I met Miquel in the Sbarro cafe, in Schipol airport (Amsterdam). Huge airport, with expensive magazines (I had to ask someone if that "15" written in the in the magazines were Euros or not). The flight to Kampala went fine. Nothing to see through the window, since it was cloudy all the time. But I could choose between lots of movies, music, games, etc on my own display. I watched The International, which I found quite good. Then Ironman. Finally, a documentary about sustainable and ecologic architecture. I was surprised with how many drinks and food we got. I lost the count, and everything was quite good. Last years I always flew in those low cost companies, and I realised the difference today. In the KLM magazine I saw a great variety of products, some digital cameras, and a ring for 500 Eur.

At the Kampala airport we got some forms from people with masks in their faces. We filled those forms saying that we have no fever, throat pain or cough. Then we got the visa. Edward, our driver, took us to the house. It took about 45 minutes on an asfalt road, with some red dust in it. It was already night, and there is almost no street lighting, so it was quite dark. The whole way smelled like burned plants. Miquel said that's very common. Some trucks driving up the hill in front of us release very black smoke, which I could smell. During the whole travel in the road we passed lots of people just walking in the road, or hanging out there. Scooters with one, two, three or even four passengers. There were many small places selling stuff. The light was almost always fluorescent tubes or energy saving lamps. Some areas had lots of scooters parked and people around them. Others crossed the smoky road running or with bicycles. In other places people would be sleeping at night, but here the whole way you would see people. I saw some playing volleyball inside a court. Somehow the drive reminded me of some road in Malaga, in a summer night.

When we arrived Robert (the guard) opened the door. The house is big, with grass around it, electric fence, very clean and new looking, and frogs and other night animals are singing out there. Time to sleep now :)

# Last video shooting day

Today was our last day out there filming. We drove back to Rakai (across the equator), to the same place where we did the circumcision filming last week. From there we drove to the country side to visit some community health workers in a AIDS help center. I showed the phone application to a doctor and two other workers while Michael controlled the camera and Sal made the questions. We received positive comments and some good ideas for new features. On the way back I could not stop taking pictures from the car. I filled my card with 150 images. I enjoyed shooting at high speed (1/500s) while I turned my body quickly to follow objects and people at the side of the road. It was very hard to aim with the car moving at 80 km/h... a fun game :)

At the equator I checked using the phones GPS feature if the line was at latitude 0º, but there was a 12 meter difference, whatever that means :)

Today we said goodbye to Bob and his son Daniel. Tomorrow early Michael and Sal (filming crew) will leave very early in the morning, and Miquel and I will fly back to Amsterdam in the afternoon. This will be the end of my first visit to this continent. Hopefully more follow :)

During the last week every evening when I get back home I'm extremely tired after so much travelling and exciting events. I only have energy now to write these lines and upload some new photos to Flickr.

# MU-JHU

Today I spent many hours with Sal and Michael Jackson doing video shootings at the MU-JHU (Makarere University - Johns Hopkins University Research Collaboration, http://www.mujhu.org/). I heard very interesting stories of how they investigate and got successful results for avoiding mother to child HIV transmission. I feel lucky to meet so many smart people in charge of projects that try to improve other people's lives.

# Muzungu

Today we visited Gaba. Edward drove us there. During the ride, as usually, we saw smoke and dust, and motorbikes carrying all kinds of stuff, in this case a large piece of glass, maybe 120cm x 120cm. A women fell from the boda-boda right in front of us when the motorbike started to move. She looked like very drunk. It took her a few seconds before she could stand up, then she walked away looking for another boda-boda, since the first boda-boda left without bothering to see if she was fine or not. It was quite shocking to see. The area where this happened is know for night life and vice.

Gaba is next to the Victoria lake. It has a market, fishermen, and people doing smoked fish. The fish are placed in large grids (like 2 meters wide), and many grids are placed on top of each other, and all grids placed inside a large container with fire and smoke. The fish come out quite black. I think then the fish (or some of it) was peeled, and the eatable parts placed in a large platform, probably to dry under the sun. On top of this platform full of fish there were two women walking bare foot. They moved the fish around, removing bad parts. Dust from the road flew over them. Some flies walked in the fish. And some huge birds sometimes got lucky and stole a piece. The women threw something at the birds to scare them, but they just jumped back, not going too far away.

I took some photos and Miquel recorded video, which he will post after editing. But I haven't taken many photos here. I don't feel so comfortable taking photos of poverty and the way people live. I don't like the fact that I can take interesting photos because of the bad luck of other people. On the other hand, it can help people in developed countries see how unequally wealth is distributed in the planet...

Suddenly, a strong rain started and everybody run quickly to find a protected place. Miquel and I knew it was coming, so we were already under a roof. A young boda-boda driver came next to me and made many questions, about the tribes in Spain and about soccer. He asked about Barça and Chelsea.

Next we drove to visit the Tombs of Buganda Kings at Kasubi. A guy gave us a great tour. He spoke very well, with an amazing voice that could have fit in a movie. Inside the old king's house he showed me how to play a game which he said was kind of old chess in Africa. It was easy to learn, but I guess hard to master. I hope I can play it again in the future. It's played on carved wood with 32 positions, and 32 seeds for each opponent. He told us about the history of Uganda, the four kings buried inside the house, and traditions.

We went to have lunch, and then we walked home crossing again the slums near our house. Immediately after entering the area, many kids started screaming "Muzungu! Muzungu!" which is the word used to refer to white foreigners. Small kids came running from every direction to see us and reapeated "hello! how are you?". They smiled and laughed. Miquel was walking behind and lots of kids started holding his hands. Later they were holding mine too. If you haven't tried dragging 7 small kids up the hill, I tell you it's quite heavy :) Maybe 20 kids walked with us all the way through the slums. I don't remember seeing such happy children ever before.

# Picking up the trash

When I was a kid, here in Finland they already separated the paper trash, and later the organic garbabe. This was maybe 15 or 20 years before it was done in Mallorca, where I grew up. Many people still don't want to "waste their time" with such things in Spain. This is very different in northern countries.

Recently I was surprised to see a sticker in one of our garbage cans in Berlin, saying that "someone threw something wrong in this container. we will not take the trash away until you fix this". If this happened in Spain the container would probably sit there full until the sun melted the container and the content away :)

Here in Soukka, until one year ago, there was a small wooden house with several trash containers inside. A different truck would come to pick up each type of garbage (paper, white glass, colored glass, organic, carton, plastic, or others). This wooden house has now been replaced by several containers that are mostly buried in the ground, probably about 5 or 6 meters deep. You use your house key to open each container and throw away your garbage. The garbage falls into a gigantic and very strong bag. Once a week, a truck with just one person comes, lifts the huge bag with an hydraulic arm, pulls from a rope in the lower end of the bag, and the content drops inside the truck. In the photo you can see half of the bag. I'm not yet sure of what benefits this system has, but I will find out.

One other interesting thing that came to my mind, is that the 10 floor building where I stay does not have any kind of communicator to let a visitor ring a neighbor, or to let someone open the building door from upstairs. During the day you can just enter the building and ring directly someone's door. But after 8pm or during weekends, the building door is locked automatically, and you can only enter if you have the key. Do people never receive visits from friends after 8pm or during weekends? If they do, they have to go down and unlock the door. Now that people have cellphones you can just ring your friend upstairs, but this system has been there for 20 or 30 years. I think people don't have so many visitors around here :)

Today I ordered a tiny video camera, so hopefully next week I can show you how beautiful Soukka is.

# School of public health

Today the video shootings continued. First with the Dean of the School of Public Health. Bob and Miquel presented him the phone and the application, which he found very interesting. After this Bob and Miquel explained to the camera the project and the technology used. It was a bit tricky because it was outdoors and the wind forced them to repeat the takes a few times. After eating a pizza we attended (and filmed) a very interesting event (the Grant Rounds) in the same campus, at the Infectious Diseases Institute (IDI). In this event, they connect the local auditorium with another one in the Johns Hopkins University in Baltimore using a satellite link. Cameras and microphones in each side transmitted live feed to the other side. 3 uncommon medical cases from Uganda were presented, the participating doctors in Baltimore tried to diagnose and give their opinions on those cases using the given data, and finally the local doctors presented what was the actual diagnosis, and interesting discoveries. So basically it was a videoconference between doctors in Kampala and Baltimore that brings new knowledge to both sides.


How did Polyphylla originate? What was
your goal in creating the instrument?
There were two elements I wanted to achieve with
Polyphylla. First, in terms of sound, I simply wanted
to create an instrument to produce the kinds of
sounds I enjoy most: complex and evolving textures.
Natural sounds are full of subtleties that change over
time, which make the sounds alive and interesting,
but unfortunately they are really hard to simulate with
digital synthesizers.
The second aspect was related to the usability of the
interface. Many of the synthesizers I have tried
included way too many parameters for my taste. This
gave them flexibility, but made them hard to work
with. Therefore I wanted to design a synthesizer that
was simpler to use without compromising its depth.
Sound visualization is an important element that is
often missing in synthesizers. I believe this can really
enhance the device’s usability by keeping the
interface engaging, easy to understand, and can
even serve as a medium to imagine new sonic
possibilities. Therefore, in the early stage of
Polyphylla’s development, I sought inspiration from
the visual world, hoping to design an approach to
audio generation with a strong visual component.
During the initial research phase, I came across
spectrograms of organic sounds and noticed
patterns that kept repeating. Fractals, together with
additive synthesis, appeared to be the logical choice
to emulate those patterns. And as it turns out,
combining them I was able to achieve my goals for
crafting complex and evolving sounds combined
with simple usability.
Can you tell me a bit about additive
synthesis?
The best analogy to additive synthesis I can think of
is a color palette - you mix a few colors together to
get a di fferent color. A similar thing happens in the
audio world as well. By mixing a few di fferent sounds
together you produce a new sound. With additive
synthesis, the sounds that get added together are
sine waves of di fferent frequencies. It’s based on the
theory of the influential mathematician Fourier, who
showed that any sound can be reproduced with a
set of sine waves of di fferent frequencies and
amplitudes. Speaking the language of additive
synthesis: when you hear a piano note of 440 Hz,
what you are hearing is only its F undamental
Frequency, but on top of the fundamental there are
h armonics which are integer multiples of the
fundamental frequency, and the noise components
which are non-integer multiples of the fundamental
frequency. These frequencies are called O vertones,
as they are tones “above” the fundamental
frequency.
As you might have noticed already, additive
synthesis provides an interesting way to think of
sound numerically. The harmony and inharmony
components in sounds are directly associated with
integer and non-integer overtones, respectively.
Pitched instruments, like piano and guitar have more
harmonic components (integer overtones), while
percussions are mainly composed of inharmonic
components (non-integer overtones).
To make it even more interesting, some instruments
tend to have more odd overtones - like the clarinet,
while the trumpet has more flat amplitude
distributions over all integer overtones. So simply by
changing the proportion of odd and even overtones
you can morph the tone between a trumpet and a
clarinet.
Furthermore, since you can control the amplitude of
each frequency individually with additive synthesis,
you can emulate how a natural sound evolves over
time. For example, when you strike a guitar chord,
the high frequency elements disappear faster than
the lower ones. Also when you strike the strings
harder, the tone appears brighter. The nature of
additive synth makes it straightforward to emulate
these behaviors.
However, to create a harmonically interesting sound
with additive synthesis, you normally need at least
100 overtones and the ability to change the
amplitude and frequency of each overtone
individually over time. This is why sound
manipulation with additive synth is notoriously
clumsy and laborious – you often need to tweak all
parameters of hundreds of partials individually.
Polyphylla preserves the power of additive synthesis
but simplifies the manipulation process with its
fractal-inspired Motion E ffects.
There’s some unique morphing e ffects in
Polyphylla - what are those doing?
The motion e ffects allow the user to draw a simple
shape, a “Motion Motif”, whose structure controls a
huge set of parameters simultaneously and
individually. The Motion Motif can be thought of as a
volume envelope, which specifies volume change
over time. For each frequency, the Motion Motif is
shifted in the time domain or deformed using
algorithms that produce apparently natural behavior.
With one free-drawing envelope and 1-4 knobs, you
can easily bring sounds to life and create many
unique subtleties that change organically over time.
T he outcome could be subtle and organic, or
dramatically abstract and wild.
With your background in programming,
how did you design Polyphylla? Was it all
Max from the start?
Initially I tested various ideas using simple paper
prototypes to avoid getting distracted by technical
details and to focus on the concepts themselves.
Once the idea of Polyphylla emerged, I started
quick-and-dirty prototyping with Max/MSP,
JavaScript, and Processing. The sound engine was
quickly assembled with a bunch of cycle~ and
envelope objects. I used Open Sound Control to
send the envelope data to Processing in which I
prototyped the visualization. Processing made it
easy to experiment with di fferent looks and
behaviors.
How did you bring it all back into Max for
Live?
Once I was certain about the direction I wanted to
build the device in, I started porting the JavaScript
and Processing prototypes to Max. Initially I tried
doing everything with Max/MSP objects, but soon I
reached a performance bottleneck due to the huge
amount of real-time data used for visualization and
harmony modulation. In order to lower the CPU
usage I rewrote the visualization and audio engine as
C/C++ externals.
Are there any particular languages/
methodologies that you used that you’d
like to bring more attention to/which
aren’t too well known?
At some point I realized I needed to build a custom
signal processing component. Instead of directly
coding a C external, I prototyped the component
using the Julia programming language. It is open
source, designed for scientific computing and has a
few handy packages for DSP and data visualization.
It allowed me to test new algorithms and
immediately view the resulting signal, down to the
sample level.
Polyphylla utilizes a unique approach to sound generation which is normally
hard to achieve with digital synthesizers. By employing mathematical models
which are based on shapes and phenomena in nature, it is easy to bring
sounds to life and create many unique subtleties that change organically over
time.
The interface is engaging and uncomplicated, thanks to its visual approach to
sound manipulation. It allows users to draw a simple shape which then
controls a huge set of parameters simultaneously. This innovative approach
has multiple benefits: it makes additive synthesis no longer clumsy and
laborious, it excels as an educational tool and it stimulates the imagination.
Polyphylla excels at evocative ambiences, moving pads, rich textures and
experimental noises - although it’s also perfectly capable of generating rich
basses and punchy leads. Please let me know if you’d like an NFR copy of
the full release for review.
Harmony Effect
Unison - fatten up the sound by adding two slightly detuned partials for each
existing one.
Detune - Adjust the detune amount to the partials colored red in the
Spectrum View.
Wet - The balance between the original partials and the detuned partials.
In the Spectrum View, the original and detuned partials are colored in white and red
respectively.
Interval - detune harmonics matching the specified interval.
Detune - Adjust the detune amount to the partials color red in the
Spectrum View.
Target - Detune All/Odd/Even/Octave/Fifth partials.
All - detune all partials
Odd/Even detunes the odd or even partials. When “Odd” is selected,
for example, the 1st, 3rd, 5th, 7th, etc. partials will be detuned.
Octave/Fifth detunes partials that match certain tuning. For example,
Octave detunes all partials that are the octaves of the fundamental frequency.
Volume - Adjust the volume of the detuned partials.
Mask - detune partials covered by the mask
Detune - Adjust the detune amount to the partials color red in the
Spectrum View.
Size - Specifies the width of the mask. With higher value to detune more
partials.
Offset - Shift the starting position of the mask.
Random - randomly detune partials.
Detune - Adjust the detune amount to the partials color red in the
Spectrum View.
Smoothness - How smooth the partials are detuned over time.
Threshold - detunes any partials with volume greater than the minimum value
Detune - Adjust the detune amount to the partials color red in the
Spectrum View.
Minimum Volume - Detune only partials with volume higher than the
specified amount
Miscellaneous Effects
Ring Modulator - Adding extra frequencies to the original signal. good for
adding extra high frequencies for percussive sounds.
Dry/Wet Mix - The balance between the dry signal and the signal
processed by Ring Modulator.
Frequency - The frequency that will be added to and subtracted from the
input signal.
Chorus - thicken the signal with the approach how a group of people
singing in choir - blend the original signal and the same signal with constantly
changing delay time
Dry/Wet Mix - The balance between the dry signal and the signal
processed by Chorus.
Feedback - The amount of processed signal feeds back into the input of
Chorus.
Thickness - Increase the value to create a thicker tone. It removes the
high frequency in the processed signal
Delay - Set the delay time of the signal. The delayed signal will interfere
with the original signal to create slight pitch variation.
Delay Time Modulation Mode - This sets how the delay time is
modulated over time. Regular Mode scales the delay time
Delay Time Modulation Amount - This sets the modulation amount
applied to delay time. Higher amount makes delay time vary in a wider range, hence
more dramatic changes of the pitch
Delay Time Modulation Rate - This sets how quickly the delay time is
modulated. Higher rate generates more rapidly varying pitch.
Reverb - enhance the original sound with/by adding natural room
acoustics.
Dry/Wet Mix - The balance between the dry signal and the signal
processed by Reverb
Size - The size of the virtual room. Larger sizes produces longer reverb
times. Smaller room size produces a more metallic tone.
Decay - The time it takes for the reflection to die off.
Damping - Makes the overall tone brighter or warmer.
Diffuse - Increase diffuse amount to put early reflections closer together.
Reduce Diffuse to create more discrete echoes.
Modulation
Modulation makes it possible to change the timbre rhythmically/randomly or to react
to the way you play your midi controller. For example, change the Brightness by how
hard you hit a key.
1. Modulation Scalar - Scale the amount of modulation applied to the associated
parameter. At the center the modulation is scaled to zero. On the right side, it scales
the original value from 0 to 1. On the left side it scales the value from 0 to -1.
2. Modulation Ring - When a Modulation Source is in use, it shows the actual value
the parameter is currently using.
3. Modulation Sources
3.1 Modulation using envelopes & LFO
The following modulation sources can be found on the lower part of the interface:
3.1.1 LFO-1 & LFO-2
3.1.2 Amp ADSR - the volume envelope for the overall sound contour. It can be used
as modulation source as well.
3.1.3 Auxiliary ADSR - an additional envelope for modulating parameters
3.2 Modulation Parameters through MIDI Controller
These modulation sources can’t be found on the interface of Polyphylla, but are MIDI
information sent from your MIDI controller.
3.2.1 Key-Tracking
3.2.2 Velocity
3.2.3 Mod-Wheel
3.2.4 Pitch-Bend
3.2.5 Aftertouch
Visualizer
The Polyphylla Motion Visualizer enables the user to work visually in designing
sounds. There are two different view options.
Graph 1
Spectrum View
Spectrum Tab shows the default energy level of all frequencies used in the current
sound. The height shows the magnitude of each frequency. Red lines indicate
detuned partials, when the Harmony Effect is enabled in the Effects tab.
Graph 2
Motion View
When a motion effect is enabled, the Motion Visualizer displays a visualization of the
magnitudes of partials evolving over time. Each curve represents one partial, with
the low frequency partials at the front and the high frequency partials to the rear.
Time progresses from left to right.
1. Sweep - offsets the start time of the Motion Motif for each partial
Amount - global dial for all Sweep Modes. It scales the amount of offset time
applied to all partials. Higher value results in more dramatic offset times
Sweep Modes
1 Down - linearly increase the offset time as the partial frequency (ie. the
Harmony Ratio) increases
2 Up - linearly decrease the offset time as the partial frequency (ie. the
Harmony Ratio) increases
3 Curve - non-linearly offset partials. The offset amount changes as a curve.
The shape of the curve can be modified by Phase and Density
Phase - shift the phase of the curve, which changes the offset amount
applied to partials
Density - set the density (also called period or wavelength) of the sweep
curve. Lower values result in more dramatic changes across the partial spectrum,
while higher values result in smoother changes.
4 Random - randomly offset partials.
Seeds - Each variety is a different set of random values to offset partials
Offset - Change the mapping between the offset amounts and the partials
2. Attractor - stretches and bends the Motion Motif with an attractor.
1. Attractor Location X-Y Control - click and drag along to change the location
of the attractor. The horizontal/vertical axis represent playback time/magnitude
respectively.
2. Offset - this changes the location of the attractor among partials
3. Deform - semi-randomly deform the Motion Motif for for each partial.
1. Deform - The amount of deform applied to all partials
2. Randomize - How random the original Motion Motif is deformed. High value
results in more dramatic timbre movement, while use low value to create more
subtle, organic timbral changes.
4. Drift - smoothly change the magnitude of each individual partial over time
1. Speed - How quickly the magnitude of individual partials changes over time
Motion Motif Editor
Click and drag to draw a curve as a Motion Motif. Depending on the Motion
Effect in use, it will be applied differently to each partial.
Presets
Factory presets for the Motion Motif
Loop Mode
This sets whether to loop the Motion Envelope for the duration of note, and if so,
how.
Speed
How many cycles per second to play back the Motion Effect.
Harmony Ratio
The Harmony Ratio of a partial is calculated in proportion to the fundamental
frequency. For example, if the fundamental frequency is 440 Hz, and the partial
frequency is 880 Hz, the partial’s Harmony Ratio is 2.
There are harmonic and inharmonic partials. Harmonic partials are partials with a
whole-numbered (1, 2, 3.. etc) Harmony Ratio. While inharmonic partials are with
non whole-numbered Harmony ratio (ex. 1.23, 1.33, etc). If the fundamental
frequency is 220 Hz, then a partial of 440 Hz is a harmonic partial, while 550 Hz is
an inharmonic partial.
With pitched music instruments like piano, guitar, or clarinet, they are composed of
mainly harmonic partials. And bell or percussions are made of inharmonic partials.

t simplifies the view. people kind of drift into seeing the world only in 2d. pictures of events and scenes are the only reason to visit the place or make the event happen in the first place. this takes it one step further. reduction to a simpler being. though there is some information loss involved in the process. we lose something. it takes the element out of context and it only makes sense to someone if he/she knows its origin. like when trying to rebuild the original from one of the 1d-ified images is not possible. we need to guess parts of it. expect parts of it. information is lost but rebuilt through memory and experience. and this is the key, its something extremely human and a unique capability of human brains. trying to rebuild the original is displaying the difference between brain and CPU.
when people take pictures they unconsciously perform a transformation: turning 3d into 2d. this project takes this concept a little further. it turns 2d images into 1d images. the original image is impossible to reconstruct, like the 3d world is impossible to reconstruct from a 2d image. this means during the transformation of taking a picture information is lost, the world is not recognizable from an image, like the image is not recognizable from the 1d representation. why would you want a representation like an image of a scene if you can perceive the reality.
technical background: the images are based on photographs of different scenes. the representation of an image in the computer is through pixels. an image consists of rows and columns of pixels and each pixels consists of three components, red, green and blue. for each row the average color of all pixels contained by that row is calculated and is set as the color for every individual pixel in that row.

So Intimate is generative and the result of the mixup of interrelated images. Combining images various ways of expression from photography, illustration, painting, visual design and funny animal pictures. The combination of those still images by overlapping results into an abstract form of a visual style that is initially set by the first image. This constructs a summary of a certain visual style that is governed by the decisions every single creator of the original images made while creating those images.
Thus, each individual image is sacrificed for the 'big image'. The need to compress data even when consuming them is rising and we can't protect ourselves from this evolutionary trend.
In a time where our consumption of images and videos raised to a new record level, the only logical step is to sum up multiple images into one, rather see the synopsis of individual processes and results instead of doing the work of finding your own interpretation of the individual processes and results. Losing control and the ability to judge with valid reason is the consequence.

In a time where consumption of media, say data, raised to a record level, the only logical step is to sum up multiple images into one, in order to quicker grasp the synopsis of what you’re looking at. The downside of this development is loosing control over ones ability to find an own interpretation of the matter, because ones decisions and thoughts are based upon the, possibly lossy, compression of the information one perceives.

The piece Abstracted Intentions puts concentrated consciousness on display. It is a generative computer performance that is fueled by humanness that has been injected into the program. Usually generative computer programs use mathematical formulas in order to simulate motion. In order to overcome this, in my perspective, cold touch that digital art has a tendency towards, all movements of the piece are guided by recorded data of my own motion. The motion is this case is something very elementary, the movements I produce with the mouse when I'm operating a computer. I am fascinated by the characteristics of these movements, they are extremely simple, as they are essentially paths with a startpoint, an endpoint and the way inbetween. At the same time these movements are infinitely complex and impossible to generatively create and artificially simulate. 
This piece is the result of a long research and experimentation phase. I experimented with various media to approach this concept and finalized on a    generative projection on top of a painting. The projection will be fast and abstract, please see the video attached. The projected lines are the same ones that are painted with thick high quality acrylic paint. It creates the sensation of augmentation of the painting, stressing it's characteristics.

go around in a neighbourhood, take random photos of things. label them with one word. write the label on the thing and take another photo of it.

take random pictures you took with your phone- at least 500. construct a 4k image by looping through the pixels and storing the color of a random picture into the current position of the output image.

in a clean room, place >4 screens where as each screen has a computer attached. on each screen there is a person on skype in real time, being open for discussion. each of the person needs to be from a different continent and in general representing all of society.

Use the MousePath libary and connect lines very closely together, so they form a big line. Keep the drawing in realtime and create a sketch that animates the sides of squares and rectangles in that way. Place 28 (invisible) rectangles on the canvas and re-draw their side-lines with the mouse paths that are close to each other. Use approximately 100 individual lines for each cube side. For this work, hand movements were extracted, reduced to their core characteristics and fed into an unrelated choreography. What is left is the feeling of pure conscious intention.
Let me give you some context, as this project is an ongoing development of finding and utilizing pure character, intention, consciousness, choice. I have been recording my own mouse movements while I am working on the computer for a couple of months now. So eventually I have collected multiple tens of thousands of movements. One movement is the path inbetween two clicks I do. 
I was led to this project by the fascination for the unconscious but yet so intended lines one draws when somebody operates the computer. The lines are all unique and each of them has some kind of concentrated characteristics containing the soul of the creator. The amount of unintendedness, that what happens when you take the lines out of their context and re-purpose them for drawing other shapes, is so strong and puts minimalistic human decision making and choice onto display.
With this endeavor I am extending myself by recording my movements in order to apply them in massive amounts, which would be impossible without this detour of recording. 
Moreover, interestingly, the characteristics of the paths you are drawing when using your computer are heavily different depending on what you are doing. The lines of working in Photoshop for an hour have essential differences to the lines of an hour of erratic Facebook research.
And by combining these concentrated 'self' into various compositions and choreographies, it is almost like i am using myself as a puppet, from a different point in time. Using 'myself' with a bigger sense of quality for the quantity. As Mohr already said in the 60s, you can not overcome a lack in quality by using more quantity. When adding an generic quantity to a system, you are adding nothing. With this work I am researching the possibillities to align the easy-to-create quantity, that enables computer generated graphics, with the quality that can not be achieved by dead, pre-determined, mathematical functions, that I see all over recent artworks, that have been created using digital tools.
Mohr already stated that for a qualitative long term work, the expressiveness of mathematical functions are limited because they are a closed system, disconnecred from human thought and feeling. The use of human intended action in a generative way, eises the quality of the quantity to a new maximum.
I have been creating a repertoire of lines i can utilize; I am directing my own movements from a distance, in a fashion of a puppet cabinet. The distance is in time and purpose. 
This video is a result of accumulated self, it is literally loaded with my intention. The medium is something non-material, my own self. 
A mousepath is concentrated information. Its human measured entropy is very high.
Static and minimalistic forms are being traced by complex line combinations, which are chosen randomly from a set of thousands of lines. 


The work "Composition #9" consists of many choreographies, which are all implementations of the umbrella composition #9. The composition is an abstract representation of the essence of my unconscious mind, putting my past movements onto display. I have been taking very detailed records of the movements I make with my mouse while using the computer, what gained me a repertoire of multi-functional lines, which represent concentrated information, traces of my past. Though, through the extreme abstraction of these movements, the underlying intentions and actions are blurred and the only thing that is left is the feeling that every line used to have an individual intention and has been created unconsciously, without any doubt or aesthetic aspirations.
What drove me to this are the fascinating characteristics of the traces of minimalistic human decision making in contrast to the deterministic qualities of mathematical functions. On the search for possibilities to combine the advantages of generative software approaches with the uniqueness of an individual hand-drawn line, I came to the point where I found out that it is impossible to synthesize these human qualities with existing geometrical algorithms or mathematical functions. Due to the predetermined qualities of mathematical functions, one function becomes a closed system and thus naturally disconnects from human thought and feeling. At the same time I was not satisfied with the thought of actually drawing myself, as this active act of intended expression blurs the overwhelming power of unconscious decision making and action.
Eventually I became aware of the fact that I need to use human generated data as the source and I started recording very detailed information about the movements of my computer cursor, while operating the machine for every day tasks, in order to re-purpose those traces for future experimentation.  With these lines I, in a way, quantified my personal motoric finger print and had the blueprint of universal human 'touch' that can possible be injected into generative systems in order to balance the weight of influence in between computer and human. Wit that I can conduct compositions and choreographies of 'myself' from a third person perspective and observe 'myself' drawing.
What you are reading and seeing is the report of a point in time of that research.

a dialogue with myself
The mouse lines are a translation, a morse code of an intention. Where as e.g. Vector Pi (https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/416797613/vector-pi-a-geometric-language-encoding) is a classical translation, which acts on the same level of information as morse, braile and, well, characters. I can not transport any more intentions of the author as a sub-tone or their characteristics as a person. It's encoding possibilities are limited and I am applying a more general and abstract translation of something yet un-measurable into that.
computer is not a tool, its an instrument
an idea is not an algorithm, its a composition

Dear Lacunians,
bear with me, this first email will probably contain a lot of bullshit, maybe there grows some interesting things out of it. This will be a lengthy mail, so only read on if you're interested in the collective exhibition next year:
I've been thinking a bit about the upcoming Lacuna Lab Exposition 2016 at Spektrum. My thoughts were mixed, when trying to come up with a way to curate or organize the thing. I don't think there can be something like that for our show, though I will try to be the one pushing it slightly forward for now and taking some responsability. 
I was wondering what we could do, do we do intentional for this occasion created work? Do we make plans to develop some things collectively? Do we just throw all of our pieces in a bowl and try to connect it? I don't know- but since there's plenty of time and Spektrum is small, there's no need to 'produce' something for that occasion. 
I'm pro taking it easy and simply be aware in the upcoming weeks/months of what you and others are doing. I kind of like the idea of having the exhibition as a snapshot of our spot, simply putting the winter onto display. Or what we've been working on in the winter. Whatever it is. A photograph of the dirty seat everybody (or is it only me?) is using to climb out of the window. Your keyboard, which is totally worn out because you've been coding like a champ. Or the lacuna logo made out of the dirt we're sweeping away from the floor. Or a piece that's been intentionally planned and created to be a work of art. The book you wrote about quantum media theory which proves the higgs-boson to be a lie.
I'll stop here (for now). I'm not sure this is the right approach for our show, but I kind of would like to stick out of this blurry pile of sometimes seemingly random stuff which is being promoted by Spektrum. Sorry, this sounds awfully ignorant, but I would like to make a point about that it would be interesting to move some things upside-down, or do something unexpected, just to keep this whole new-media arts thing a little spicy.
Another thing, in case we need funding for anything we would like to do there (finally print a pile of dog shit in gold) there are probably some ways to get some funding from somewhere. It would probably be good to have that e.V. shit sorted by then, but I'm in general positive regarding our ability to figure out the ordinary problem of having too little cash for something supercool. Of course, if anybody ever hears about any opportunities like that- holler.

The painting gets this extraordinary computational touch. It's entirely human and fluid, has no mathematical foundation at all. At least no very obvious one, so it's obfuscated. But the arrangement of the lines creates a gradient that is mathematically perfect. Impossible to find that in nature. And I was not able to create this kind of perfection with my hands either. I could reach it with a machine though.

Paint a painting with a gradient from left to right. The gradient is calculated by the average color of all pixels in y direction. A gradient that goes from left to right, white to black. Still randomly chosen paths, but alternately painted black and white, still woven highlights.
The painting gets this extraordinary computational touch. It's entirely human and fluid, has no mathematical foundation at all. At least no very obvious one, so it's obfuscated. But the arrangement of the lines creates a gradient that is mathematically perfect. Impossible to find that in nature. And I was not able to create this kind of perfection with my hands either. I could reach it with a machine though.

Why do I need to learn the technique of machine learning?
I need to transfer myself to a machine in order to learn what I am. This enables me to look at myself from the outside and reflect on my thoughts, actions characteristics.

The painting is a dialogue with myself on a low level. While recording my own bodies movements, I extract some alternative DNA of myself into data. This information about lines and movements can be used to inject a character into any kind of algorithmic composition to transcend it. These lines are a translation of abstract intentions, having had a former purpose but at the same time stand for themselves, expressing the the core of ones personality. 
This painting is heavily influenced by computational and digital means. The computer is not a tool, it's an instrument. An idea is not an algorithm, it's a composition.

Dear everybody,
I've already been speaking to a few of you, this idea creating some anonymous-collaborative project, that we could use for our exhibition. It's an idea where it is really important that _everybody_ gets involved.
What it essentially boils down to is the need for 10 pages of text from everybody. This text will then be fed into a recurrent neural network layed out for text. After this is done it'll be possible to extract text/poems from this that reflects all of our output. This already exciting enough to do it, isn't it? What follows after that will be up to us.
Important is that this text comes from YOU. It doesn't matter weather it's outgoing emails, diary entries, notebook notes about projects etc- all these should be sufficient to get some (semi/abstract) meaningfull output. 
As it might be slightly sensitive text, I came up with a way we can organize this. Say everbody who wants to join in on this, has their text ready, not everybody might be comfortable with sending this text via email to anybody (especially not to me most probably). Regarding this, I suggest to use this encryption technique: 
1. Encrypt your text via  https://encipher.it/
2. Save the password(!)
3. Send me the encrypted text via mail. It's going to look something like this:
EnCt268ef7fe91b58d5200ecc99c07cea8d3db73b0c2868ef7fe91b58d5200ecc99c0V6vPsNmxOQD UFCWxYVbo6m48IwEmS
Decrypt it at https://encipher.it
4. Once I have all encypted texts, we'll have to all meet in person in order for everybody to decrypt their text, so the network can be trained on the real text. The plain texts will then directly, after the network has been trained, be deleted. So nobody will ever be able to read your text.
5. Done. The network contains the texts, which can not be re-extracted. What will be possible is to sample the network for text that is deeply influenced by all of our input. Wicked!
There's a talk here by Andrej Karpathy, who's doing lots of scientific research with deep neural networks, in case you're interested in some very techy explaination:  https://skillsmatter.com/skillscasts/6611-visualizing-and-understanding-recurrent-networks
